Here is a 300-400 word abstract for the given research article:

**Title:** Unveiling the Role of GL-1/Notch Signaling in Cell Reprogramming in Caenorhabditis elegans**

**Research Question:** How does the GL-1/Notch signaling pathway facilitate reprogramming of germline cells into somatic cells in Caenorhabitis elegans, and what are the underlying molecular mechanisms?

**Methods:** We employed a combination of genetics, tissue-specific transcriptome analysis, and functional studies of candidate genes to investigate the role of GL-1/Not signaling in reprogramming.

**Findings:** We discovered that GL-1/Not signaling promotes reprogramming by activating genes silenced by the Polycomb repressive complex 2 (PRC2) and identified the conserved histone demethylase UTX-1 as a crucial GL-1/Not target facilitating reprogramming.

**Implications:** These findings have significant implications for our understanding of development and diseases associated with abnormal Notch signaling.

**Conclusions:** Our study reveals an unexpected role of GL-1/Not signaling in reprogramming, contrary to its known function in maintaining undifferentiated germl stem/progen cells. We propose that GL-1/Not promotes reprogramming by activating specific genes silenced by PR2, and identify UTX-1 as a key GL-1/Not target facilitating reprogramming.

**Key findings:**

* GL-1/Not signaling is essential for reprogramming germl cells into somatic cells
* GL-1/Not activates genes silenced by Polycomb repressive complex 2 (PR2)
* UTX-1, a conserved histone demethylase, is a crucial GL-1/Not target facilitating reprogramming
* GL-1/Not and PR2 have antagonistic effects on cell fate decisions
* GL-1/Not regulates genes maintaining germl stem/progen cells

**Implications:**

* Understanding GL-1/Not role in reprogramming has implications for development and diseases associated with Notch signaling
* GL-1/Not may be a target for therapeutic intervention in Notch-related disorders

**Future directions:**

* Investigate GL-1/Not reprogramming in other organisms
* Explore GL-1/Not regulation of PR2 and UTX-1 in other contexts
* Investigate GL-1/Not role in Notch-related diseases

**References:**

* Patel et al. (2011)
* Kim and Critenden (2007)
* Fong et al (2002)
 Bender et al (2004)
 Kner et al (2014)

**Title:** The Adaptor Protein SK2 (SK2) is Essential for Protection against Klebsiella pneumoniae ATCC 438 Pulmonary Infections

**Research Question:** What is the role of adaptor protein SK2 (SK) in the host defense against Klebsella pneumoniae ATCC 438 pulmonary infections?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of SK2 in the innate immune response to Klebsella pneumoniae ATCC 438 using a murine model of pulmonary infection. SK- mice were compared to wild-type mice in terms of bacterial burden, neutrophil function, and reactive oxygen species (ROS) production.

**Findings:** SK- mice had a 100-fold higher bacterial burden compared to wild-type mice. Neutrophils and monocytes were present in lungs, but neutrophil degranulation was normal in response to Kleella pneumonia infection. However, Kle pneumonia-stimulated ROS production was abolished in vitro. Kle pneumonia-induced neutil ROS required the activity of Src family kinases (SFK), Syk, Btk, PLC2, and PK. The loss of SK significantly hindered Kle pneumonia-induced phosphorylation of FK, Sy, and Py2, implicating SK as proximal to their activation in pathogen signaling pathways.

**Implications:** SK-dependent neutil signaling is essential for Kle pneumonia-activated ROS production and promoting bacterial clearance during infection. The sensitivity of pneumonia to ROS is supported by increased bacterial burden in mice and observations in chronic granulomatous disease (CGD) patients.

**Conclusions:** SK plays a critical role in host defense against Kleella pneumonia AT 438 in pulmonary infections. SK is indispensable for pneumonia-stimulated neutil ROS, but not degranulation or phagocytosis. The increased burden in SK- mice is due to loss of ROS sensitivity. These results indicate pneumonia 16 is resistant to neutil phagocytosis regardless of SK expression, but elicits SK-independent degranulation during infection. ROS is a mechanism for protection against pneumonia in mice lacking the gp91ph component of NAD oxidase.

**Title:** Modeling the Dynamics of Plasmodium vivax Relapse Infections: A Within-Host Model of Liver-Stage Hypnoites and Its Implications for Malaria Transmission

**Research Question:** How do liver-stage hypnoites contribute to Plasium viv relapse infections and malaria transmission, and what are the implications for malaria control strategies?

**Methods:** We developed a within-host mathematical model of liver-stage hypnoites (hypnoites) and validated it against data from tropical strains of P. vivax. The model was then embedded in a P. viv transmission model to demonstrate the build-up of the hypnoite reservoir following new infections and its depletion through hypite activation and death.

**Findings:** Our results show that the hypite reservoir is overdispersed, with many individuals having few or no hypites and some having intensely infected livers. Individuals with more hypites are predicted to experience relapses and contribute to P. viv transmission. Incorporating hypite killing drugs, such as primaquine, into first-line treatment regimens is predicted to cause substantial reductions in P. viv transmission, as individuals with most hypites are likely to relapse and be targeted for treatment.

**Implications:** Relapses arising from hypite activation in the liver have significant consequences for P. viv transmission dynamics. The hypite reservoir constitutes a third parasite reservoir, in addition to blood and mosquito reservoirs for P. falciparum. Relapses can be incorporated into Ross-Mac models through a state representing the hypite reservoir. In the absence of diagnostics for liver-stage parasites, models will play a crucial role in estimating effectiveness of interventions targeting the hypite, either via vector and blood-stage anti-malarials or directly primaquine.

**Conclusions:** Our within-host model of hypites demonstrates that many epidemiological patterns of relapse can be explained by assuming hypites activate and die at a constant rate. This is integrated into existing Rossac models to account for rel infections of P. viv malaria, providing insights into impact of control on parasite reservoirs in vector, blood, and liver. Our results highlight the importance of incorporating hypite dynamics into malaria models for effective control and elimination strategies.

**Title:** Post-Logging Carbon Recovery in Amazonian Forests: A Regional Patterns and Predictions of Carbon Sequestration Potential

**Research Question:** How do regional differences in climate, soil, and initial biomass affect carbon sequestration in Amazonian forests after selective logging, and what are the key drivers of post-disturbance recovery?

**Methods:** We employed a novel approach using a network of 133 permanent forest plots (175 ha) across Amazonia to link regional differences in climate, soil, and initial biomass with carbon fluxes to provide Amazon-wide predictions of post-logging recovery. We analyzed long-term (8-30 year) data from 13 experimentally-disturbed sites in the Amazon and Guiana Shield.

**Findings:** Our results show that aboveground carbon recovery over 10 years is higher in the Guiana Shield (21.3 ± 3 Mg ha-1) than in the south (12.3 ± 3 Mg ha-1) where environmental stress is high (low rainfall, high seasonality). We highlight the key role of survivors in forest regrowth and elaborate a comprehensive map of post-disturbance carbon sequestration potential in Amazonia.

**Implications:** We partitioned contributions to post-disturbance carbon gain (growth and recruitment of ≥20 cm DBH) and loss from mortality of survivors and recruited trees to detect main drivers and patterns of recovery in forests disturbed by selective logging across Amazonia. Our study provides clues on regional differences in Amazonian response to large losses by other disturbances (e.g., wildfires, droughts). We upscale results to Amazonia using maps of climate, conditions, and soil density to elaborate predictive maps of changes over 10 years under a 40% loss, a common intensity after logging.

**Conclusions:** Our study highlights the importance of considering regional differences in climate, soil, and initial biomass when predicting post-logging carbon recovery in Amazonian forests. The Guiana Shield shows higher carbon sequestration potential than the south due to lower environmental stress. Survivors play a crucial role in forest regrowth, and our maps provide a comprehensive understanding of post-disturbance carbon potential in Amazonia.

**Title:** Distinguishing Quiescent Neural Stem Cells from Neurosphere-Initiating Cells in the Adult Ventricular Subventricular Zone**

**Research Question:** The relationship between quies neural stem cells (NSCs) and neurosphere-initiating cells (NICs) in the adult ventricular subventricular zone (SVZ) remains unclear.

**Methods:** We prospectively identified and isolated NICs from the adult mouse SVZ using flow cytometry, characterizing them as GlastEGhighPlexBhighCD24/lowO/4AAM/lowTer/119/45 (GEOT) cells, which were highly mitotic and short-lived in vivo. In contrast, pre-GEOT cells were quiescent, expressed higher Glast, and lower EGFR, and PlexB.

**Findings:** GEOT NICs formed neurospheres, expressed stem markers Slc1-CERT, GF-CERT, Sox2ERT, and GliERT, and were long-lived in vivo. In contrast, pre-GEOT cells could not form neuropheres, were quies in vivo, but long-lived in the SVZ based on fate mapping with stem markers. Conditional deletion of Bmi-1 polyprotein depleted pre-GE and GEOT cells, with pre-GE cells more dependent on B-1 for Cdk2 (p16ink4) repression.

**Implications:** Our data distinguish quies NSCs from NICs, enabling study of their properties in vivo.

**Conclusions:** We identified two phenotypically and functionally distinct populations of neural stem/progenitor cells in the adult SV: GEOT cells, highly mitotically active and transient, and pre-GE cells quies and long-lived. GEOTs formed neuropheres, expressed stem markers, while pre- cells did not. Conditional deletion of B-1 protein depleted pre- and GE cells, with pre cells more dependent on B- for Cdk2 (16ink) repression. Our findings distinguish quies NSCs from NIC, enabling study of their properties in vivo.

**Title:** Piezo1 Channels Behave as Independent Mechanotrans at Physiologically Relevant Densities, Enabling Homogeneous Force Transduction Across the Cell Membrane

**Research Question:** How do Pie1 channels spatially interact and functionally modulate each other's mechanical sensitivity at various densities, and what are the implications for force transduction across the cell membrane?

**Methods:** We employed cell-attached patch-clamp electrophysiology and pressure-clamp to investigate Pie1 channels in cells expressing endogenously low and overexpressing Pie1 at high levels. Stochastic simulations of Pie1 spatial distributions were used to predict channel overlap at endogenous (1-2/μm2) and non-physiological (10-100/μm) densities.

**Findings:** Our data, together with simulations, show that Pie1 channels at physiologically relevant densities (1-2/μm) behave as independent mechanotrans, with no effect on pressure sensitivity or open probability in the absence of membrane tension. In contrast, non-physiological densities (10-100/μ) exhibit substantial overlap, suggesting functional modulation of each other's sensitivity.

**Implications:** This property is essential for cells to transduce forces homogeneously across the entire membrane, as Pie channels have no intrinsic tendency to spatially concentrate and functionally modulate each other's mechanical sensitivity.

**Conclusions:** Pie1 channels, at physiologically relevant densities, inherently function as mechanotrans, enabling cells to transduce forces with spatial homogeneity across the membrane. This is crucial for cells to detect and respond to mechanical forces in their environment.

**Title:** Investigating the Effects of Volatility on Decision-Making: A Novel Insights from a Bounded Accumulation Model**

**Research Question:** How does the accumulation of noisy evidence to a threshold influence decision-making, particularly in terms of speed, accuracy, and confidence?

**Methods:** We employed a novel sensory manipulation that mimics the addition of unbiased noise to motion-selective regions of the visual cortex, verified with neuronal recordings from macaque areas MT/MST, to test predictions of a bounded accumulation model.

**Findings:** Increasing noise led to faster decisions, reduced accuracy, and paradoxically higher confidence in both humans and macaques over a range of stimuli with minimal accuracy impairment. The magnitude of effects agreed with predictions of bounded accumulation model.

**Implications:** This study provides evidence for the bounded accumulation model's ability to account for joint effects of volatility on choice, reaction time, and confidence.

**Conclusions:** A manipulation that increases the variance of momentary evidence on a decision (volatility) increases speed and confidence associated with it. Testing volatility's influence on decision-making is challenging due to independent control over signal and noise in evidence. We mimicked manipulation by changing statistical properties of a dynamic stimulus, verified by recording from direction-selective neurons in MT and MST areas of macaque cortex. Neurons in these areas represent momentary evidence in tasks identical to our study (Salzman et al., 1990; Celebrini & Newsome, 199; Ditter et al., 2003; Fets et al., 2014).

**Title:** Regulation of Caenorhabditis elegans Lifespan by Insulin Signaling and Calcineurin Pathways

**Research Question:** How do the insulin and calcine signaling pathways regulate lifespan in Caenorhabditis elegans, and what is the role of the transcription factor DAF-16 in this process?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of the calcine phosphatase TAX-6CN-1 and the Ca2+/calmodulin-dependent kinase II (CAMK) ortholog UNC-43 in regulating DAF-16, the ortholog of the mammalian FOXO transcription factor, in Caenorhabditis elegans. We used RNA interference (RNAi) to knockdown of daf-16, tax-6, and unc-43, and observed the effects on DAF-16 localization and lifespan.

**Findings:** We found that UNC-43 phosphorylates and activates DAF-16, promoting its nuclear localization, while TAX-6CN-1 dephosphorylates D-16, leading to its inactivation. Our data suggest that CAMK (UNC-43) and calcine (TAX-6CN-1) regulate Caenorhabitis lifespan through reversible phosphorylation of D-16 at serine 286. The antagonistic nature of these enzymes raised the possibility that their opposing effects on D-16 are negated by a common signal, Ca2/CAM.

**Implications:** Our results indicate that the insulin and calcine pathways regulate Caenoritis lifespan through D-16, with insulin signaling dominating when there is a conflict between the two pathways. Multiple pathways, including insulin/IGF-1, gerine, mitochondrial, and dietary restriction regulate aging in Caenorhabitis, with the best characterized being the insulin pathway. Our study highlights the complex regulation of D-16 by CAMK, calcine, and insulin signaling in Caenoritis.

**Conclusions:** The calc phosphatase TAX-6-1 and CAMK UNC-43 regulate Caenor lifespan by phosphorylating and dephosphylating D-16 at serine 286. The antagonistic nature of these enzymes suggests that their opposing effects on D-16 are negated by a common signal, Ca/CAM. Insulin signaling appears to dominate when there is a conflict between the two pathways. Our study contributes to understanding the complex regulation of aging in Caenoritis and the role of D- in this process.

**Title:** Hierarchical Organization of Motor Programs Underlies Sequential Grooming Behavior in Drosophila melanogaster

**Research Question:** How do nervous systems implement the sequential execution of motor programs to achieve complex behaviors, such as grooming, and what are the organizational principles governing this process?

**Methods:** We employed genetically targeted activation of neural subsets to drive distinct motor programs for cleaning individual body parts in Drosophila melanogaster, allowing us to experimentally control and reveal the hierarchy of motor programs.

**Findings:** Our results demonstrate that motor programs are organized in a suppression hierarchy, with earlier programs suppressing those that occur later. Cleaning one body part reduces sensory drive to its motor, relieving suppression of next movement, allowing sequence to progress down hierarchy. A model featuring evoked movements in parallel, but selected through hierarchical suppression, reproduces the grooming sequence.

**Implications:** This study provides the first example of an innate motor sequence implemented by a prevailing model for generating action. Our findings suggest that evolutionarily ancient ability to select competing behaviors through hierarchical suppression may have provided infrastructure to perform actions in sequence.

**Conclusions:** This study reveals the organizational principles underlying neural control of serial selection in an innate behavior common to arthropods, grooming. Our empirically derived model demonstrates how suppression mediates sequential selection of motor programs. We anticipate that identifying neural circuitry for each module and their interaction will reveal how serial behavior is encoded in nervous system through hierarchical mechanism.

**Title:** Shugoshin-like 2 (Sg2) Regulates Chromosome Segregation by Coordinating Cohesion, Spindle Assembly Checkpoint, and Kinetochores-Microtubule Interactions

**Research Question:** How do diverse processes involved in accurate chromosome segregation, including cohesion, spindle assembly checkpoint, and kinetochore-microtub interactions, coordinate during meiosis I in mammalian oocytes?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of Shoshin-like 2 (S2) protein in regulating these processes in mammalian oocytes.

**Findings:** Our study reveals that S2 plays a crucial role in turning off the spindle assembly checkpoint (SAC), promoting bi-orientation of bivalents on meiosis spind, facilitating kinetoch-microtule (Kibers) formation, and limiting bivalent stretching. S2's ability to protect cohesin depends on interaction with PP2A, while its ability to silence SAC is mediated by direct binding to Mad2. In contrast, its effect on bivalent and Kiber formation is independent of PP2 and mediated by recruitment of MCA and inhibition of Aurora kinase activity.

**Implications:** S2 links multiple processes essential for faithful chromosome segregation by virtue of its multiple interactions. Depletion of S2 in oocytes has pleiotropic consequences during meiosis, including inefficient SAC silencing, increased bivalent stretching, and alterations in kinetoch-microtule attachments. We show S silences SAC by binding to Mad2 using a MIM motif common to Mad's partners Cdc20 and Mad1. Delayed biorientation of bival on meiosis spind, increased bivalent, and Aurora/C activity at kinetores, accompanied by increase in lateral and decrease in kinoch-microt attachments. S2 deficiency delayed APC/CC20 activation, suggesting it required to shut SAC.

**Conclusions:** A single protein regulates sister chromatid cohesion, chromosome traction, spindle checkpoint, and Aurora/Cases. Demonstrating different mutations alter regulation but not processes, we prove pleiotropy in S2/Δ oocytes not due to secondary or tertiary effects eliminating single function. We show S2 defective PP binding fails protect centrom cohes, as S1 in yeast (Xu et al, 2009). However, if sole function S2, chromosome behavior meiosis should unaffected. To our surprise, S2 deficiency striking changes chromosome microt dynamics.

**Title:** Persistence of Experience-Dependent Changes in Brain Connectivity Requires RNA Localization and Protein Synthesis: A Role of Netrin-1/DCC Signaling in Synaptic Translation

**Research Question:** Do experience-dependent changes in brain connectivity require localized RNA and protein synthesis, and what are the underlying mechanisms that regulate this process?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of local translation and RNA trafficking in sensory-motor neurons using imaging and molecular biology techniques. Cultured Aplyia sensory-motor neurons were stimulated, and RNA localization and translation were imaged. Mechanisms of local translation were investigated, including calcium-dependent netrin-1/DCC signaling.

**Findings:** RNAs were delivered throughout the arbor of sensory neurons, but translation was enriched at sites of synaptic contact and stimulation. Calcium-dependent netrin-1/DCC signaling triggered local translation. Spatial regulation of gene expression was mediated by local translation rather than RNA delivery from the nucleus to synapses. Three mRNAs, ribosomal proteins, and translation factors were delivered to both synaptic and non-synaptic sites, but translation was enriched at sites of contact and stimulation.

**Implications:** These findings suggest that spatial regulation of gene expression in sensoryotor neurons is primarily at the level of translation, not RNA targeting from nucleus. Netrin-1 release from SN or MN during synapse formation and plasticity triggers translation at synaptic but not non-synaptic sites. Recombinant netrin-1 incubation with Aplyia sensoryotor cultures demonstrated regulation of localized transcripts.

**Conclusions:** This study demonstrates that experience-dependent changes in brain connectivity require localized RNA and protein synthesis, and that netrin-1/DCC signaling is a key regulator of this process. Netrin-1 release from SN or MN during synapse formation and plasticity triggers translation at synaptic but not non-synaptic sites. These findings have implications for understanding synaptic plasticity, learning, and memory.

**Title:** Investigating the Morphogenesis of Human Induced Pluripotent Stem Cell Colonies through CRISPR-Meduced Knockdown of Cortical Tension and Cell-Adhesion Regulators

**Research Question:** How do cell-cell interactions and multicellular organization in human induced pluripotent stem cell (hiP) colonies be controlled through the manipulation of molecular regulators of cortical tension and cell adhesion?

**Methods:** We employed an inducible CRISPR interference (CRPR) system in hiSC to silence key proteins regulating cell adhesion and cortical tension. We created mixed populations of hiSC with and without CRPR system, then induced mosaic knockdown (KD) of ROCK1 and CD1 using CRPR.

**Findings:** Mosaic knockdown of ROCK1 or CD1 resulted in differential patterning within hiSC colonies due to self-organization, while retaining epithelial pluripotent phenotype. Knockdown induced a transient wave of gene expression within mixed populations stabilized with observed self-organization. Mosaic patter enabled interrogation of emergent multicellular properties, facilitating understanding molecular pathways regulating symmetry-breaking morphogenesis.

**Implications:** This study identifies a novel strategy to control emergence heterotypic cell populations and multicellular organization in pluripotent cells, creating robust models for mechanistic study human tissue patterning formation.

**Conclusions:** Our results demonstrate that CRPR-induced knockdown of cortical tension and adhesion regulators in hiSC subpopulations leads to controllable self-organization and patterning. This approach enables interrogation of emergent multicellular properties, providing insights into molecular pathways regulating symmetry-breaking during morphogenesis. Mosaic KD of ROCK1 or CD1 results in 'bottom-up' cell-driven formation of hiSC colonies, preserving pluripotency. This study opens avenues for directed emergence of heterotypic populations and multicellular organization in pluripotent cells, facilitating mechanistic study of developmental patterning and tissue formation.

**Title:** A Normative Theory for Set Effects in Visual Working Memory: Minimizing Error and Neural Cost

**Research Question:** How does the number of items encoded in visual working memory affect precision, and what is the underlying mechanism behind this effect?

**Methods:** We propose a normative theory that balances expected performance against the cost of neural resource spent on encoding, and construct a model based on this theory. We test the model on data from nine delayed-estimation experiments, examining effects of set size and probability on precision.

**Findings:** Our model accounts for effects of set size and probability on precision, and predicts a non-monotonic relation between set size and total resource invested in encoding. We also show that it is sometimes optimal to encode a subset or none of items in a task, raising the possibility that cognitive limitations arise from cost minimization rather than constraints.

**Implications:** Our theory provides a principled explanation for set effects in working memory and attention, and may apply beyond these domains. It formalizes the speculation that selectivity arises from balancing performance against spiking costs, and explains size effects in attention tasks. The model has few parameters and accounts for nonotonicity between size and resource invested in encoding.

**Conclusions:** Our findings support a normative theory that minimizes error and neural cost, providing a new understanding of set effects in working memory and attention. The model's predictions can be tested in neuroimaging experiments, such as Bal et al. (2016). Our theory also makes predictions about incentive manipulations on precision, as they affect the expected cost function.

**Title:** Pathogen Manipulation of Autophagy: A Novel Mechanism of Plant-Pathogen Interaction

**Research Question:** How do plant pathogens interfere with autophagy processes, and what are the implications for plant defense against infectious diseases?

**Methods:** We investigated the interaction between the Irish potato pathogen Phytophthora infestan's effector, PRD54, and the autophagy protein ATG8 in Arabidopsis thaliana.

**Findings:** PRD54 binds to AT8 with high affinity, stimulating AT8-marked autophagosome formation and depleting the autagy receptor J2 out of AT8 complexes, thereby interfering with J2's positive effect on pathogen defense.

**Implications:** This study reveals a novel mechanism of plant-pathogen interaction, where a pathogen effector has evolved to antagonize a host autagy receptor to counter host defenses.

**Conclusions:** Our results show that pathogens can activate autagy for their benefit, promoting nutrient recycling and eliminating defense-related compounds. This study highlights the complexity of plant-pathogen interaction and the need to consider pathogen-directed modulation of autagy and cellular trafficking during infection.

**Title:** Elucidating Cardiac Fibroblast Responses to Simultaneous Environmental Stimuli: A Novel Insights into Pathological Mechanisms of Cardiac Fibrosis

**Research Question:** How do cardiac fibroblasts (CFs) respond to simultaneous environmental changes in mechanical strain and oxygen tension, and what are the implications for cardiac fibrosis?

**Methods:** We employed a multi-stimulus microdevice to apply cyclic strain and controlled oxygen to primary human cardiac fibroblasts (CFs) to investigate their responses to individual and combined stimuli.

**Findings:** Our results show that CFs exhibit differential responses to various combinations of mechanical and oxygen stimuli, with individual stimuli inducing proliferative effects (PH3 mitotic cells, YAP translocation, PD secretion) or collagen presence. Notably, the combination of hypoxia and simulated loss of contractility (2% strain) triggers increased release of inflammatory and pro-fibrotic cytokines and matrix metalloproteinases.

**Implications:** This study proposes a novel model recreating a pathological environment for understanding CF responses during cardiac injury, replicating for the first time the combination of oxygen and mechanical cues, revealing how these stimulations combine to trigger pathological responses and emerging cellular mechanisms. Our findings have implications for in vitro models of cardiac disease, where CFs have been studied under physicochemical stimulation such as mechanical stress, oxygen deprivation, and biochemical with proibrotic cytokines.

**Conclusions:** This study provides insights into the synergistic contributions of environmental signals in the activation of early CF responses relevant to cardiacrotic disease. After ischemic myocardial insult (e.g., acute artery occlusion), tissue oxygen levels drop to 5-6 O2. During proliferative myocardial healing, excessive CF proliferation and phenotypic switch to fibrot response. To understand environmental modulation of CF proliferation, we examined mitotic cells (PH3+/DA) under mechanical and oxygen changes. We found mimicking combination of H and contractility crucial in eliciting inflammatory and fibrot remodeling of CF, while individual stimuli regulated proliferative and collagen responses. Our model provides a tool for understanding pathological mechanisms and tailoring reparative strategies.

**Title:** Alternative Isoforms of the DOM Nucleosome Remodeling ATPase, DOM-A and DOM-B, Specify Distinct Multiunit Complexes for Transcriptional Regulation in Drosophila

**Research Question:** How do the alternative isoforms of the DOM nucleosome remodeling ATPase, DOM-A and DOM-B, contribute to transcriptional regulation in Drosila, and what are the mechanisms underlying their distinct functions?

**Methods:** We investigated the functional roles of DOM-A and DOM-B in transcriptional regulation using a combination of biochemical and genetic approaches, including RNA-seq analysis, knockdowns of DOM-A, DOM, T60, and H2V, and biochemical assays for histone acetylation and ATPase activities.

**Findings:** We found that DOM-B incorporates H2V, the fly ortholog of H2, genome-wide in an ATP-dependent manner, similar to the yeast SWR complex, while DOM-A functions as an ATP-independent histone acetylase complex similar to yeast Nu4, targeting lysine 12 of histone H4. Our data suggest that DOM-A might serve as the central subunit of a Nu4-type complex in Dophila, and that ATP activity of DOM is dispensable for H4 acylation by DOM-A.

**Implications:** Our work provides an instructive example of how different evolutionary strategies lead to similar functional separation in yeast and humans, nucleosome and histone acetylase complexes originate from gene duplication and paralog, while Dophila generates diversity by alternative splicing a single gene. This study highlights the importance of alternative splicing in generating functional diversity amongst chromatin regulators.

**Conclusions:** Our findings demonstrate that DOM-A and DOM specify distinct multiunit complexes for transcriptional regulation through different mechanisms, with DOM-B incorporating H2 in an ATP-dependent manner and DOM-A functioning as an ATP-independent histone acetylase. This work illustrates how alternative splicing can generate functional diversity amongst chromatin regulators and highlights the complexity of chromatin regulation in Dila.

**Title:** Unraveling the Role of β-Catenin (Ctn1) in Motor Neuronal Differentiation: A Retrograde Signaling and Transcriptional Regulation

**Research Question:** What are the functions of β-Caten (tn1) in motorneuronal differentiation, and how does it regulate presynaptic formation through retrograde signaling?

**Methods:** We employed an in vivo transgenic approach to dissect the functions of β-Caten (tn) in motoruronal differentiation. We generated transgenic mice expressing wild-type or mutant βaten with impaired in transcriptional regulation or cell-adhesion signaling. We analyzed presynaptic morphology and function in these mice.

**Findings:** Our results show that βaten without the transactivation domain (TAD) was unable to rescue presynaptic deficits of βaten mutation, indicating the involvement of transcriptional regulation. In contrast, cell-adhesion function of βaten was dispensable. We identified Slit2 as a potential βaten-directed retrograde factor and showed that Sl2 expression in muscle diminished presaptic deficits in βaten mice. Sl2 immobilized on beads induced synapsin puncta in axons of spinal explants.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that βaten regulates presaptic differentiation through transcriptional control of muscle-derived proteins. This is supported by recent reports that stable βaten expression in muscle impairs presaptic in mutant mice. We propose that βaten in muscle is critical for a retrograde pathway directing terminal development.

**Conclusions:** βaten in muscle regulates presaptic differentiation by controlling the expression of muscle-derived proteins. Our study highlights the importance of transcriptional regulation in βaten function and identifies Slit as a potential retrograde factor. These findings provide new insights into the mechanisms of motoruronal differentiation and presaptic formation.

**Title:** Force Transmission at Cell-Cell Adhesions Regulates Tissue Development and Maintenance

**Research Question:** How do mechanical forces transmitted through cell-cell adhesions influence tissue development and maintenance in epithelial tissues?

**Methods:** We employed a combination of computational and experimental approaches to quantify force transmission at both subcellular and multicellular scales in spontaneously forming adherent epithelial cell clusters. We used a generalized cell-cell force measurement method to analyze spatially and temporally the transmission of mechanical forces through cell-cell junctions of dynamic epithelial clusters.

**Findings:** Our time-resolved force measurements demonstrated that cell-cell forces at junctions fluctuate over time, both during interphase and cell divisions. We found that basal fluctuations were coupled to E-cadher localization at the level of individual-cell junctions. At multicellular scale, cell force transmission depended on cell position within cluster and was adaptive to reconfigurations due cell divisions or positional rearrangements. Importantly, force transmission through cell required coordinated modulation cell-matrix adhesion and actomy contractility in cell and its neighbors.

**Implications:** These findings provide insights into mechanisms controlling mechanical homeostasis in epithelial tissues and highlight our methods as a resource for study mechanotransduction cell adhesions. Our results suggest that force transmission through cell-cell adhes plays a crucial role in tissue development and maintenance.

**Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates the importance of force transmission cell-cell adhes in epithelial tissues and highlights the need for further investigation into mechanotransduction mechanisms. We provide a novel method for quantifying force transmission cell-cell adhes at subcell and multicellular scales, which can be applied to study various biological processes, including tissue morphogenesis, wound healing, and disease.

**Key Findings:**

* Force transmission through cell-cell junctions fluctuates over time, both during interphase and cell divisions
* Basal fluctuations are coupled to E-cad localization at individual junction
* Multicellular force transmission depends on cell position within cluster and is adaptive to reconfig due cell divisions or positional rearrangements
* Force transmission through cell requires coordinated modulation cell-matrix adhesion and actomy contractility in cell and neighbors
* Our method provides insights into tissue mechanics, revealing force transmission across cell is short-ranged, typically on scale one cell diameter due anchoring forces by cell-matrix adhes and actomy contractility

**Title:** Elucidating the Structural and Biochemical Properties of Huntingtin and the Impact of Polyglutamine Expansion in Huntington's Disease

**Research Question:** How does the polyglutamine expansion in the huntingtin protein contribute to the pathogenesis of Huntington's disease, and what are the structural and biochemical properties of the full-length huntingtin protein affected by this expansion?

**Methods:** We employed a combination of biophysical experiments, including circular dichroism, single-particle microscopy, and cross-link mass spectrometry to investigate the structural and biochemical properties of huntingtin and the effect of polyglut expansion.

**Findings:** Our results reveal that huntingtin is composed of five distinct domains and adopts a spherical α-helical soloid structure, with the amino-terminal and carbox-terminal regions forming a circumscribed central cavity. Notably, polyglut expansion increases α-hical properties of hunting and affects intramolecular interactions among domains.

**Implications:** This study delineates the structural characteristics of full-length huntingtin affected by polyut expansion, providing an elegant solution to the conundrum of how the amino-terminal polyut tract confers novel property on hunting, causing disease. Our findings suggest that hunting may undergo conformation change upon interaction with partners.

**Conclusions:** Our systematic biophysical and biochemical analyses of a panel of purified recombinant huntingins with varied polyut tracts provide insight into the impact of polyut on hunting basic features. **Title:** Hierarchical Organization of the Lateral Prefrontal Cortex: Unveiling the Apex of Higher Cognition

**Research Question:** Does the lateral prefrontal cortex (LP) exhibit a hierarchical organization, with progressively rostral areas processing increasingly abstract information and facilitating efficient cognition?

**Methods:** We employed functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) and dynamic causal modeling to investigate the functional organization of the LP in humans. We examined the rostral-caud axis of the LP by analyzing gradients of stimulus domain-specificity and temporal control, and modeled interactions among LP sub-regions to assess hierarchical processing.

**Findings:** Our results revealed rostral-caud gradients of abstraction in the LP, with dynamic modeling indicating asymmetrical interactions indicative of hierarchical processing. Contrary to assumptions, the strength of efferent versus afferent connections positioned the midLP as the apex of the hierarchy. Cognitive demands modulated connectivity towards LP, consistent with a role in integrating information control. Furthermore, strengths of these dynamics were related to higher-level cognitive ability.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest the LP is hierarchically organized, with the midLP positioned to synthesize abstract and concrete information for behavior. The organization of LP is intimately tied to cognitive capacity, with top-down control within LP a determinant of higher function. Asymmetrical projections from midLP to other LP regions were related to higher-level ability, indicating a beneficial effect of hierarchical organization with midLP at the apex.

**Conclusions:** This study provides evidence for hierarchical organization of the LP, with midLP at the apex, contrary to dominant assumptions. Our results suggest top-down influences from rostral to midLP and bottom-up from caud to midLP during cognitive engagement. Effective connectivity and modulations indicate midLP forms a nexus where information converges to action. These findings have implications for understanding LP function and its role in higher cognition.

**Title:** Elucidating the Role of Ubiquitination in the Transcriptional Activity of the Coactivator NPR1 in Plant Immune Response

**Research Question:** How does ubiquitination of the coactivator NPR1 contribute to its transcriptional activity and immune response in plants?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of ubiquitination in the transcriptional activity of NPR1, a key coactivator in plant immune response, using Arabidopsis thaliana as a model organism. We examined the involvement of Cullin-RING E3 ligase (CRL) and E4 ligase U4 in ubiquitination and proteasomal degradation of NPR.

**Findings:** Our results show that ubiquination of NPR is a progressive event initiated by CRL, which promotes chromatin association and expression of target genes. However, ubiquitination by U4 leads to proteasomal degradation of NPR. Conversely, ubiquitin ligases UBP/7, proteas-associated deubiquitinases, enhance NPR longevity. Immune transcriptome reprogramming requires sequential actions of E3 and E4 ligases balanced by deubiquases fine-tuning NPR activity without sacrificial turnover.

**Implications:** Our findings indicate that CRL-mediated ubiquination is required for NPR to attain full transcriptional activity, while stepwise formation of ubiquitin chains by U4 inactivates NPR and promotes degradation. Initial ubiquination boosts NPR transcriptional coactiv activity by enhancing promoter occupancy and potentially genomic mobility.

**Conclusions:** Our study reveals that ubiquitination of NPR plays a crucial role in plant immune response. Unlike E4 ligases, which extend pre-existing ubiquitin chains, E3 ligases contribute to initial ubiquination of substrates. In Arabidopsis, E4ase U4/M3 is involved in degradation of NLR immune receptors. We propose that ubiquitination itself acts as a transcriptional signal, providing a window for NPR to activate target genes. Our study demonstrates that ubiqu chain extension and trimming can fine-tune transcription outputs of unstable activators without sacrificial turnover.

**Title:** Emergence of Mature Neural Networks and Spatiotemporal Activity Patterns Supports Cognition: A Developmental Regulation and Evolutionary Conservation**

**Research Question:** How do large-scale synaptic and cellular activity patterns develop across the postnatal course of neural networks to perform complex computations?

**Methods:** We employed high spatiotemporal in vivo electrophysiology in immature mice to investigate the progression of large-scale and cellular activity across development.

**Findings:** Our results reveal that cortical processes emerge rapidly and simultaneously after a discrete but volatile period at the beginning of the second postnatal week of rodent development. This transition is characterized by neural quiescence, followed by spatially distributed, temporally precise, and organized activity. We demonstrate a similar developmental trajectory in humans, suggesting an evolutionarily conserved mechanism facilitating transition network operation.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that transient quies period is a requisite for the emergence of coordinated networks. The transition between immature and mature dynamics is marked by decreased cellular and synaptic activity at the beginning of the second week, followed by precise synchronization and oscillatory in space and time.

**Conclusions:** The maturation of cortical dynamics is preceded by a discrete, evolutionarily conserved period during early development. During this period, oscillations and spiking display a nad signals shift from local, loosely correlated, sensory-driven patterns to internally organized, spatially distributed, and temporally activity. We hypothesize that emergence of advanced network properties is heralded by disappearance of immature patterns. Cortical microcircuits undergo dramatic changes in anatomical connectivity and functional during this epoch, with an abrupt increase in synaptogenesis in superficial layers. Neural spiking is more efficiently recruited to oscillatory with strong phase-locking and higher precision, characteristic of adult networks engaged in information processing. A transient developmental perturbation may be an evolutionarily indicator of substantial shift in cortical operations.

**Title:** Mechanism of Deamidation-mediated Inhibition of UBC/TRAF6 Complex Formation and Suppression of NF-κB Inflammatory Response by Shigella flexneri Effector OI

**Research Question:** How does the deamidation of glutamine residue Q100 in the ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme UBC by Shella flexner effector OI affect the interaction between UBC and the RING ubiquitin ligase TRAF6, leading to suppression of the NF-κ inflammatory response?

**Methods:** We investigated the effects of deamation on the interaction between UBC and TRAF6 using biochemical assays and molecular dynamics simulations.

**Findings:** Deamation of Q100 in UBC creates a new intramolecular saltbridge that competes with a critical intermolecular saltbridge at the native UBC/TR6 interface, preventing transient interactions necessary for complex formation. Additionally, repulsion between the negatively charged E100 and the negatively charged surface of TR6 reduces transient interactions in the UBC/TR6 complex.

**Implications:** Our findings reveal a novel mechanism by which post-translational modification perturbs the conformation and stability of transient complexes to inhibit protein-protein association. Deamation disrupts the interaction between UBC and TR6 by three mechanisms: i) intramolecular saltbridge in U13 competes with intermolecular saltbridge in native complex, ii) saltbridge competition perturbs U/TR6 transient to inhibit association, and iii) repulsion between negatively charged E and negatively surface TR6 reduces transient interactions.

**Conclusions:** Shella flexner effector O suppresses the inflammatory NF-κ pathway via deation of UBC at Q100. Deation inhibits the synthesis of K-linked polyubitin chains by the U/TR6 complex, preventing downstream activation of NFκ pathway. Our results highlight the importance of understanding post-transl modifications in regulating protein-protein interactions and their impact on cellular signaling pathways.

**Title:** Paradoxical Effects of Optogenetic Manipulation on Cortical Interneuron Functions in the Auditory Cortex of Awake Mice

**Research Question:** Can optogenetic activation and inactivation of specific cortical interneuron types provide consistent insights into their computational roles in the auditory cortex?

**Methods:** We used single-unit recordings in the auditory cortex of awake mice to optogenetically manipulate somostatin-positive (S+) and parvalbum-positive (Pb+) interurons while recording responses to tones. We evaluated the effects of activation and inactivation on response gain, tuning, and information transfer.

**Findings:** Inactivation of S+ interurons increased response gain, while inactivation of P+ interurons weakened tuning and decreased information, implying these neurons support separable functions. However, activation of S+ and P interurons revealed no differences.

**Implications:** Our results challenge the notion that activation and inactivation will produce consistent insights into interurons' computational roles. We propose that small changes in parameters, such as spontaneous activity or light strength, determine whether activation and inactivation produce consistent or paradoxical conclusions about interurons functions.

**Conclusions:** Our study highlights the complexity of using causal strategies to gain insight into interuron functions. We demonstrate the importance of considering multiple factors, such as spontaneous activity and light strength, when interpreting optogenetic data. Our findings suggest that S and P interurons have distinct roles in the auditory cortex, but activation and inactivation do not reveal these differences. We propose a simple model to understand this asymmetry, showing how small changes in parameters can lead to paradoxical conclusions about interurons' functions.

Our results indicate that entraining activity within the frontop network enhances WM, modulates regional activity in a phase-dependent manner, and correlates with behavioral performance. Functional connectivity is modulated in a phase-dependent manner, with frontal interactions increasing with synchronous and posterior with desynchronous stimulation. This demonstrates the role of phase in cognitive WM processes and neural correlates.

**Title:** Fertilization-Triggered Auxin Production and AGL62-Mediated Transport Drive Seed Coat Development in Flowering Plants

**Conclusions:** Our study provides new insights into the neural codes of the R, a critical for both spatial and nonpatial behaviors. Integration of parallel streams of information in downstream neurons within ipsilateral and contralateral granular and granular retrosial cortices is crucial for a mechanistic understanding of retrosial computations. Further research is needed to elucidate how these streams are integrated.

**Title:** Unraveling the Complexity of Brain Connectivity: The Role of Spatial Arrangement of Cortical Regions in Modelling Brain Function and Behavior

**Research Question:** How does the spatial arrangement of cortical regions interact with measures of brain connectivity, and how does it relate to trait variability in behavior and lifestyle across individuals?

**Methods:** We employed resting-state functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to investigate the relationship between the spatial organization of functional regions and functional connectivity in the brain. We used partial correlation analysis (CCA) to disentangle the influence of spatial organization from coupling between neural populations.

**Findings:** Our results show that the shape and location of brain regions strongly interact with modelling brain connectivity, and that spatial arrangement of functional regions is predictive of non-imaging measures of behavior and lifestyle. We found that cross-sub variations in spatial configuration of functional regions are often interpreted as changes in functional connectivity, highlighting the importance of understanding these effects when interpreting functional imaging data and cognitive traits.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that spatial organization of functional regions is a significant confound for studies of functional connectivity, and that disambiguating its influence enables estimation of fMRI measures that uniquely reflect coupling between neural populations. Recent advances in spatial alignment have tackled the issue of misalignment across individuals.

**Conclusions:** The organization of the brain into large-scale networks has been extensively investigated using resting-state fMRI. Spontaneous fluctuations in brain regions measured with fMRI show correlations, revealing patterns of functional connectivity (FC) (Biswal et al., 1995; Frist, 1994, 201). Our partial CCA results show network matrices contain unique trait-level cross-sub information not reflected in spatial organization of functional regions. The conceptualization of brain organization is relevant to interpretation of findings. The implication is that cross-sub information in adopted 'connectivity' largely reflects variability in location of regions rather than coupling strength, for methods mapping group-level parcellations onto data.

**Title:** Reevaluating the Role of TBP2 in Skeletal Muscle Regeneration and Gene Expression

**Research Question:** Does TBP2 play a crucial role in skeletal muscle regeneration and gene expression, as previously proposed, or is TBP the essential component of the pre-initiation complex (PIC) that promotes muscle gene expression in differentiated muscles?

**Methods:** We investigated the expression of TBP and TBP2 in myotubes derived from C2C12 myoblasts and primary mouse satellite cells (MuSC), and analyzed the regeneration potential of T2 deficient mice. We also compared the RNA profile of Tbp and Tbp2 genes in MuSC isolated from skeletal muscles of wild-type mice and C2 myogenic cell line.

**Findings:** Our results show that TBP, not T2, is the essential component of PIC that promotes muscle expression in skeletal muscles. T2 is not expressed in myotubes from C2 mylast or primary MuSC. T2 deficient mice displayed intact regeneration, and Mu isolated from T deficient muscles differentiated into myubes with same efficiency as wild-type Mu.

**Implications:** Our findings demonstrate the lack of requirement of T2 for adult muscle regeneration, prompting us to compare RNA profile of Tbp and T2 genes in Mu isolated from skeletal muscles of wild-type mice and C myogenic cell line. T expression was detected in Mu-derived myubes and C12 myubes (Figure 2). Our results indicate that TBP is essential for muscle differentiation and absence of T2 in myubes suggest that TID complex may be present in myogenic and drive gene transcription during mylast into myubes.

**Conclusions:** We conclude that TBP, not T2, is required for muscle gene expression in skeletal muscles. T2 does not replace T during differentiation, as previously proposed. TID subunits and T are downregulated during myoblast differentiation, forming a complex detectable on muscle promoters essential for their expression. Our data demonstrate that TBP is essential for muscle expression, while T2 is not.

**Title:** Large-Scale Optogenetic Stimulation Modulates Excitability of Neural Circuits and Drives Neuroplasticity: Implications for Neurorehabilitation

**Research Question:** How does large-scale optogenetic stimulation modulate the excitability of neural circuits and drive neuroplasticity in the brain, and what are the implications for neurorehabilitation?

**Methods:** We used a large-scale optogenetic interface to simultaneously stimulate excitatory neurons and record electrocortic activity across primary somatosensory (S1) and motor (M) cortex in macaques. We tracked two measures of network connectivity: the response to focal stimulation and baseline between pairs of electrodes, which were strongly correlated before stimulation. Within minutes, stimulation in S or M significantly strengthened functional connectivity between areas. At a finer scale, stimulation led to heterogeneous changes across the network, reflecting correlations introduced by stimulation-evoked activity, consistent with Hebbian plasticity.

**Findings:** Our results demonstrate a robust relationship between stimulus-evoked correlations and changes in connectivity across the network, explained by a Hebb model of large-scale-induced plasticity. We found that stimulation-evoked activity drives large-scale plasticity in a Hebb manner, with significant implications for future neurorehab strategies.

**Implications:** This work extends Hebb plastic models to large circuits, with significant implications for stimulation-based neurorehabilitation. Our findings support a key role for Hebb learning in sensorimotor cortical plasticity, but highlight the need for further study of intermediate timeframes and cortical mapping. The large interface enabled robust estimation of functional and stimulation-evoked correlations.

**Conclusions:** Large-scale optogen stimulation modulates excitability of neural circuits and drives neuroastic, with implications for neurorehab. Our results demonstrate a robust relationship between stimulus-evoked correlations and changes in connectivity, consistent with Hebb plasticity. We show that stimulation-ev activity drives large-scale plasticity in a Hebb manner, informing future neurohab strategies. This work extends Hebb models to large circuits, supporting a key role for Hebb learning in sensorotor plasticity.

**Title:** Understanding the Neural Consequences of Primary Visual Cortex Lesions: A Case Study of a Rhes Macaque Monkey Born Without V1 and Implications for Rehabilitation

**Research Question:** What are the neural consequences of damage to the primary visual cortex (V1) on visual perception and behavior in ahes macaque monkey born without V1, and how can this understanding inform the development of rehabilitation methods?

**Methods:** We investigated ahes macaque monkey born without V1 using multi-modal imaging techniques, including magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) and functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to examine the structure and function of visual areas outside the lesion. Visual stimulation with checkerboards and full-field dots activated the lateral geniculate nucleus and pulvin, respectively, while visual cortical activation was sparse and included patches. Functional connectivity analysis revealed an intact network of bilateral dorsal visual areas temporally correlated with V/MT activation, even without V.

**Findings:** Despite robust subcortical responses to visual stimulation, we found little evidence for strengthened subort input to V/MT supporting residual function or blindsight phenomena. Visual structures both cortical and subort outside the lesion remained intact with little atrophy, suggesting a role in residual function consistent with an intact network of extrastate visual areas comparable to controls. Connectivity between subort regions and V/MT was weak, but microstructure intact.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that residual vision can manifest in different ways, including ability to determine information from faces, as seen in patient TN (Bur et al., 2013), which has been suggested to survive loss V. For motion stimuli, we saw activation in region V/ in dorsal ST of hemispheres, suggesting activation may be subthreshold and difficult to detect. Without strong visually-evoked V/ responses, we mapped a functional network of areas showing similar BOLD response to V/MT. Retinal information may reach dorsal areas via weak pathways LG and pul, but lack of consistent activation due to anesthesia makes question difficult. Lack of differences between S and controls in pathways LG-V/ and pul-V/ suggests either could support residual abilities.

**Conclusions:** This study provides insights into the neural consequences of primary cortex damage on visual perception and behavior inhes macaque born without V, informing the development of rehabilitation methods. Our findings suggest that residual vision can manifest in different ways, including ability to determine information from faces, and that retinal information may reach dorsal areas via weak pathways. The lack of significant differences between S and controls in pathways LG-V and pul-V suggests either could support residual abilities. These results have implications for the development of rehabilitation methods for patients with similar damage.

**Title:** Unveiling the Molecular Mechanism of Innate Immune Sensor IF16's Ability to Distinguish between Self and Foreign DNA in the Nucleus

**Research Question:** How does the innate immune sensor IF16 distinguish between self and foreign double-stranded (ds) DNA in the intracellular environment, particularly in the nucleus?

**Methods:** We employed a combination of biochemical and single-molecule techniques, including time-resolved bulk and single-molecule fluorescence assays, to investigate the molecular mechanisms underlying IF16's ability to recognize and respond to foreign DNA.

**Findings:** Our results demonstrate that IF16 scans along exposed stretches of foreign dsDNA to assemble into supramolecular platforms, while chromatin barriers prevent IF16 from targeting host DNA by interfering with this one-dimensional movement. This unique scanning-assisted assembly mechanism enables IF16 to distinguish between self and foe and assemble oligomers efficiently on foreign DNA.

**Implications:** Our findings provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms of innate immunity, resolving a long-standing question in immunology. We show that IF16's 1-diffusion mediated assembly plays a key role in regulating its immune responses, allowing a limited number of molecules to target large genomes of invading pathogens.

**Conclusions:** Our study reveals a unifying mechanism explaining IF16's central role in initiating foreign-dsensing pathways. We propose that 1-diff mediated assembly is essential for IF16's ability to distinguish self from foreign in the nucleus, and that chromatin acts as a key feature preventing IF16 from targeting host DNA. These findings have implications for understanding the innate immune system's ability to recognize and respond to pathogens, particularly in the context of viral infections such as herpes simplex-1.

**Title:** Spatially Targeted Optical Proteomics (ST): A Novel Technique for Interrogating Micron-Scale Regions of Interest in Mammalian Tissue without Genetic Manipulation

**Research Question:** Can a novel proteomics technique, Spatially Targeted Optical Proteomics (ST), be developed to interrogate micron-scale regions of interest (ROIs) in mammalian tissue without genetic manipulation, fixation, and formalin, or methanol?

**Methods:** Methanol-fixed specimens are stained with fluorescent dyes or antibodies to visualize ROIs, then soaked in solutions containing photo-tag 4-benzylbenz-glyc-histidine. Confocal imaging with two-photon excitation is used to covalently photo-tag all proteins within ROI to a resolution of 0.67 µm in the xy-plane and 1.48 µm axially. After solubilization, photo-tagged proteins are isolated by mass spectrometry.

**Findings:** As a test case, amyloid plaques in an Alzheimer's disease (AD) mouse and post-mortem case were examined, confirming known constituents and discovering new ones. ST can be applied to various samples, including cell lines, primary cultures, ex vivo, specimens, biopsy, and post-mort tissue.

**Implications:** ST offers single-mic-scale three-dimensional resolution, an order of magnitude better than current state-of-the-art LCM, providing clues to neurodegenerative disease etiology and advancing research in cell biology by yielding small features not amenable biochemical purification.

**Conclusions:** ST combines two-photon laser microscopy with photochemical labeling and mass spectrom, previously used in microscopic 3D-resolved photochemistry. The predominant photo-tagged protein is Aβ, not visible in non-plaque experiment due to low abundance. The photo-tag procedure uses two-ph microscopy, not conventional confocal microscopy. ST represents first application to affinity labeling, offering single-mic-scale resolution.

**Title:** Disruption of Frizzled3 (Fz) Gene Disrupts Axonal Growth and Survival in Motor Neurons: Implications for Planar Cell Polarity Signaling in Axon Guidance**

**Research Question:** How does the disruption of the Frizz3 (F) gene affect axonal growth and survival in motor neurons, and what is the role of planar cell polarity (PC) signaling in axon guidance?

**Methods:** We investigated the effects of Frizz3 (F) gene disruption on axonal growth and survival in motor neurons in the VIIth and XII cranial nerves, phrenic nerve, and dorsal nerves of fore- and hindlimbs in mice. We analyzed the phenotypes of Fr3−/− limbs and found that dorsal axons stall at a precise location in the plexus, distinct from other axon-pathfinding mutants, and do not reroute to alternative trajectories. We also examined the fate of affected motor neurons and found that they die 2 days prior to normal developmental cell death, coinciding with innervation of muscle targets.

**Findings:** Our results provide in vivo evidence for the idea that developing neurons with long axons are programmed to die unless their axons arrive at intermediate targets on schedule. We found that the absence of Fr3 leads to specific stalling among LM axons in the limb, distinct from phenotypes produced by other signaling systems. Affected cranial and spinal neurons die shortly after axons stall, suggesting that growing axons receive essential survival signals from intermediate targets. We ruled out aberrant pathfinding as the primary cause of motor deficiency in Fr3−− mice, as most axons do not follow erroneous trajectories.

**Implications:** Our findings implicate planar cell polarity (PC) signaling in axon growth and highlight the role of PC proteins in cell-cell complexes in epithelia in dynamic axonal growth. We propose that controlling ax growth may be the primary function of Fr3 in motor neurons, as a subset of motor neurons exhibit both ax growth defects and apoptotic death in Fr− embryos. Our results support the model of neuronal survival factors produced by intermediate targets along ax's trajectory, as suggested by in vitro co-culture experiments.

**Conclusions:** Disruption of the Frizz (F) gene disrupts axonal growth and survival in motor neurons, highlighting the importance of planar polarity signaling in axon guidance. Our findings provide evidence for the idea that developing neurons with long axons are programmed to die unless axons arrive at intermediate targets on schedule, and that growing axons receive essential signals from targets for survival. These results have implications for understanding axon guidance and neuronal development, and the role of PC proteins in cell-cell complexes in epithelia.

**Title:** Unraveling the Complex Evolution of C4 Photosynthesis: A Polyphyletic Origins and Flexible Trajectories

**Research Question:** How did C4 photosynthesis evolve independently in at least 60 plant lineages, and what are the underlying evolutionary drivers of this complex trait?

**Methods:** We conducted a meta-analysis of 18 lineages containing C3, C4, or intermediate C3-C forms of photosynthesis to parameterize a 16-dimensional phenotypic landscape. We developed and experimentally verified a novel Bayesian approach based on hidden Markov model to predict the evolution of C4 phenotype.

**Findings:** Our study reveals that the polyphyletic appearance of C4 photosynthesis is associated with diverse and flexible evolutionary paths that group into four major trajectories. We found that the order of C trait acquisition is flexible and driven by non-photosynthetic drivers, facilitated by ancestral and initial phenotypic alterations unrelated to photosynthesis.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that the emergence of C4 phenotype is not solely driven by photosynthetic pressures, but rather by non-photosynthetic drivers. This flexibility has facilitated the convergent evolution of this complex trait. We propose that early evolutionary events determined downstream phenotypes of C sub-types by restricting lineages to independent pathways across phenotype space.

**Conclusions:** Our study provides insights into the evolution of one of the most complex traits to have arisen in lineages: C photosynthesis. We conclude that distinct sub-types evolved as a consequence of alternative evolutionary responses to non-photosynthetic pressures. The mechanistic diversity underlying multiple pathways generating C photos may be key in facilitating convergent evolution of complex traits. This has implications for efforts to recapitulate acquisition of C photos through genetic engineering of C species, expanding the molecular toolbox to establish C traits in distinct backgrounds.

**Title:** Unveiling the Role of Nucleotide Specificity in the Function of SpoIVA, a Bacterial Cytoskeletal Protein

**Research Question:** How does the specificity of nucleotide hydrolysis influence the function of SpoIVA, a bacterial cytoskeletal protein that polymerizes during Bacillus subtilis sporulation?

**Methods:** We reengineered the nucleotide-binding pocket of SpoIVA to mimic its ancestral GTPase activity, creating SpoAGase, and investigated its function in vitro and in vivo. We also examined the effects of varying concentrations of ATP and GTP on SpoIVA and SpoAGase polymerization.

**Findings:** SpoAGase functioned as a GTPase but failed to polymerize due to lack of NDP intermediate formation required for polymerization. Incubation with ATP did not promote polymerization, indicating that nucle base specificity, not just energy from hydrolysis, is crucial for function. Spo and SpoAG polymerized in presence of ATP, but not GTP, suggesting specific requirement for nucle base. Physiological levels of intracellular ATP, not GTP, facilitated conformation changes in Spo and SpoAG. Both Spo and Spo failed to undergo hydrolysis-mediated changes in presence 0.06 mM GTP, but did so in 1.5 mM ATP.

**Implications:** This study reveals that nucle specificity of hydrolysis is critical for Spo function, not just energy released from hydrolysis. Increased levels of ATP relative to G at end of sporulation may have driven the change in nucle preference in Spo from GTP to ATP.

**Conclusions:** Our findings suggest that nucle specificity of hydrolysis, in addition to energy, plays a key role in biological functions. Spo, a cytoskeletal protein that polymerizes during Bacillus subtil sporulation, has evolved to use ATP instead of GTP, likely due to selective pressure of physiological levels of intracellular ATP during sporulation. This study highlights the importance of considering nucle specificity of hydroly in understanding protein function and evolution.

**Title:** Long-term Consequences of Early Leptin Signaling Deficiency on Metabolic and Neuroendocrine Development

**Research Question:** What are the long-term effects of early leptin signaling deficiency on metabolic and neuroendocrine development, particularly during critical periods of growth and development?

**Methods:** To investigate the role of leptin in early life, we restored leptin signaling at the 4th or 10th week of age in mice null for the leptin receptor (LepR) gene. **Findings:** We found that defects previously considered irreversible due to neonatal leptin deficiency, such as poor arcuate nucleus projections, were recovered by Lep reactivation in adulthood. **Implications:** Our findings reveal that early defects in leptin signaling cause permanent metabolic, neuroendocrine, and developmental problems. Leptin may be involved in developmental programming (Ravski and Horvath, 2015). Early postnatal lept block in rats leads to long-term lept resistance and susceptibility to diet-induced obesity (Att et al, 2008; Licio et al,200; Donato et,201).

**Conclusions:** The importance of lept signaling during development for long-term homeostasis and aspects regulated by lept is not fully understood. Our study uncovers the long consequences of absence leptin in early life. Lep deficiency leads to reduced brain mass, disruption of projections from AR neurons to paraventricular nucleus of hypothalamus (PV), behavioral and cognitive problems. Our findings contribute to understanding metabolic programming of metabolic, neuroend, and developmental consequences from absence leptin in life, especially in context of alarming childhood obesity worldwide (Cunningham et,201).

**Title:** Unveiling the Complexity of the Arabidopsis Circadian Clock: Single-Cell Oscillations and Cell-to-Cell Coupling across the Plant

**Research Question:** How does the Arabidopsis circadian clock regulate gene expression across the day-night cycle, and what is the level of robustness of the clock in individual cells, and how is timing coordinated across the plant?

**Methods:** We examined the activity of the Arabidopsis circadian clock at the single-cell level across Arabidopsis seedlings over several days under constant environmental conditions using a reporter for the core gene CIRCAD CLOCK ASSOCIATED 1 (CA1) (Wang and Tob, 1998).

**Findings:** Our data reveal robust single oscillations, albeit desynchronized, with two waves of clock activity, one up and down the root, and evidence of cell-to-cell coupling, especially in the root tip. A simple model shows that cell-to-cell coupling and measured differences in period between cells can generate the observed waves in gene expression.

**Implications:** Our results reveal the spatial structure of the plant clock and suggest that unlike the centralized mammalian clock, the Arabid clock has multiple coordination points. We estimate the strength of coupling between cells and find evidence of strong coupling, especially in the root. A simple model suggests that observed differences in period plus cell-to-cell coupling can generate the observed waves in clock expression.

**Conclusions:** Our data reveal both the structure and robustness of the plant circadian system. However, our results suggest that the structure of the clock is more complicated, as the hierarchical model does not explain observed short oscillations in the tip or wave of expression from the root. Our results support a decentralized model of coordination in plants (Endo, 2016; Endo et al, 2014). Our findings are consistent with previous studies arguing that oscillations are uncoupled (Thain et al,2012) and provide new insights into the dynamics of the Arabid clock at single resolution.

**Key Findings:**

* Single-cell oscillations are robust and desynchronized
* Two waves of clock activity, one up and down the root
* Evidence of cell-to-cell coupling, especially in the root
* Simple model shows cell-to-coupling and period differences can generate waves
* Decentralized model of coordination in plants supported
* Uncoupled oscillations consistent with previous studies

**Title:** Identifying Neural Biomarkers for Multitalker Speech Intelligibility in Adults with Clinically Normal Hearing

**Research Question:** Can we develop objective physiological markers to predict multalker intelligibility in adults with clinically normal hearing?

**Methods:** We conducted a study involving over 100,000 participants with clinically normal hearing, who visited a clinic due to reduced hearing difficulties. The correlation between low FM encoding and intelligibility may reflect fast processing fidelity and intelligibility. We aim to develop objective markers for complaints of poor communication in clinically normal hearing individuals.

**Title:** Unveiling the Critical Role of PALB2 in Homologous Directed Repair (HDR) through DNA Binding and Interactions**

**Research Question:** How does the PAL2 protein, a partner of BRCA1 and BR2, contribute to homologous directed repair (HDR) and interact with chromatin proteins, and what is the functional significance of its DNA binding?

**Methods:** We investigated the DNA-binding sites of PAL2 and its impact on RAD51 foci formation and HDR efficiency in cells. We also examined the role of PAL2's N-terminal DNA-binding domain (NDB) in stimulating RAD51 recombinase activity and strand exchange reactions.

PAL2's NDB stimulates RAD51 recombinase activity, including strand exchange without RAD51, and can use RNA as a substrate. **Implications:** PAL2's interaction with DNA is crucial for BR2 and RAD51 recruitment to DSBs and efficient repair in cells. PAL2 NDB stimulates RAD51-mediated strand exchange in vitro. Our results demonstrate PAL2's novel activity and importance in DNA maintenance.

**Conclusion:** PAL2, a partner of BR1 and BR2, is a tumor suppressor linked to malignancies, including breast cancer and Fanconi anemia. PAL's interaction with DNA is essential for BR2 and RAD51 recruitment to DSB and efficient repair in cells.

**Title:** Divergence in the Pleiotropic Floral Regulator APETALA1 (AP) Contributes to Species-Specific Petal Robustness in Arabidopsis thaliana and Cardamine huta

**Methods:** We investigated the role of the pleiotropic floral regulator APETALA (AP) in the divergence of petal number between the two species using a combination of genetic and molecular approaches.

**Findings:** Our results show that divergence in AP1 accounts for the species-specific difference in petal robustness. However, AP polymorphisms do not directly contribute to within-species variation Cuta pet number. Instead, decimation of pet in A reflects robustness to genetic variation. Complementing AP mutants with AP genomic from Cuta mimicked variable pet found Cuta, suggesting hidden variability in the regulatory network controlling pet number A.

**Conclusions:** Our study highlights the importance of AP in the evolution of petal robustness between species. AP is a key regulator of flower development, and its divergence contributes to species-specific petal number. Our findings have implications for understanding the genetic basis of phenotypic variation and the evolution of complex traits.

**Title:** The Nucleic Acid-Binding Activity of SUVH1 Contributes to Heterochrom Assembly and Targeting

**Title:** Decoding Position in Space: Grid Cells in the Entorhinal Cortex Respond to Covert Attention without Physical Movement

**Research Question:** Do grid cells in the entorinal cortex require physical movement to elicit spatially structured receptive fields, or can they be activated by covert attention?

**Methods:** We recorded neuronal activity from 141 neurons in the entorinal cortex of two rhes macaque monkeys performing a covert attention task, where they maintained central fixation while attending to a small dot moving in the periphery. Monkeys performed the task with >70% accuracy, with most errors due to saccades outside the fixation window.

**Findings:** We identified a significant population (20/14%, p<0.05) of entorinal cells with spatially structured fields, contrasting with recordings in the hippocampus where grid-like representations were not observed. Our results provide evidence that entor cells in macaque do not rely on physical movement.

**Implications:** Our findings support the hypothesis that grid cells participate in a range of cognitive functions, as attention is an important control for mental processes (Peters and Posner, 2012). Grid cells may be activated by movements of attention, not just physical movement.

**Conclusions:** Activation of entor cells with spatially firing fields does not require physical movement through environment. Previous studies demonstrated stationary grid cells can be activated by eye movements as monkeys explore complex scenes (Killian et al, 2012). Our results provide strong evidence that entor cortex can represent not only gaze but locus of attention in general. The neural substrate for overt and covert attention is largely overlapping (Corbetta et al,199).
