**Title:** Establishing Reproducibility in Rodent Behavior: A Standardized Task for Decision-Making in Mice Across Laboratories**

**Research Question:** Can a standardized task for decision-making in mice be reproduced across multiple laboratories, and what are the implications for reproducibility in neuroscience?

**Methods:** We developed a perceptual value-based decision-making task for head-fixed mice and standardized training protocol, hardware, software, and procedures. We trained 140 mice across seven laboratories in three countries and collected 5 million choices into a publicly available database.

**Findings:** Learning was variable across mice and laboratories, but once training was complete, there were no significant differences in behavior across laboratories. Mice in laboratories adopted similar reliance on visual stimuli, past successes, and failures, and estimates of stimulus probability to guide choices.

**Implications:** These results reveal that complex behavior can be reproduced across laboratories, establishing a standard for reproducible rodent behavior and providing an unprecedented dataset and open tools to study decision-making in mice. They indicate a path toward achieving reproducibility in neuroscience through open-science approaches.

**Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates the feasibility of reproducible rodent behavior across laboratories, suggesting a path toward improving reproducibility in neuroscience. By adopting identical equipment and protocols, mice learned the task in all laboratories, and performed comparably after learning. Mice in laboratories adopted comparable reliance on visual stimuli, past, successes, and estimates to guide choices. To facilitate reproducibility, we adopt an open science approach, sharing hardware, software, and protocols. Our results provide a large cohort of mice trained in task, demonstrating reproducible methods and results across laboratories.

**Significance:** Physiological studies of decision are increasingly carried out in mice to leverage genetic, imaging, and physiological tools for brains. Our collaboration aims to explore neural basis of behavior in multiple laboratories, enabling comparison of results, knowing differences unlikely due to behavior. We hope these resources catalyze new adaptations and variations, accelerating use in high-quality, reproducible studies of neural correlates decision.

**Key Takeaways:**

* Standardized task for decision-making in mice is reproducible across laboratories
* No significant differences in behavior after learning across laboratories
Mice rely on visual, past, successes, and estimates to guide choices
Open science approach facilitates reproducibility
Large dataset and tools for decision-making in mice
Path toward reproducibility in neuroscience

**Title:** Divergent Sensory Mechanisms in Mormyrid Fish: A Novel Coding of Electric Signals through Oscillatory Synchrony**

**Research Question:** How do evolutionary adaptations in sensory perception relate to physiological coding of stimuli in mormyrid fish, and what mechanisms underlie the divergence in sensory capabilities?

**Methods:** We investigated the peripheral coding of electric signals in mormrid fish, which communicate through species-specific electric discharges (EOD) with variable interpulse intervals (I). We compared the responses of two types of receptors: spiking, sensitive to waveform variations, and oscillating, producing spontaneous oscillatory potentials.

**Findings:** Oscillating receptors respond to electric pulses by resetting their phase, resulting in transient synchrony among receptors that encodes timing and location, but not waveform. These receptors are sensitive to frequencies found in collective signals of conspecifics, correlated with increased behavioral responses to these frequencies. In contrast, spiking receptors are specialized for detecting signals from individuals.

**Implications:** Our findings provide the first evidence for sensory coding through oscillatory synchrony, a novel mechanism where receptors with spontaneous oscillatory activity respond to stimuli with phase resets, resulting in transient synchrony across the population. The degree of synchrony provides information about pulse timing, but not waveform, consistent with the inability of fish to behaviorally detect EOD variation.

**Conclusions:** We hypothesize that divergent mechanisms represent adaptations for different social environments. The oscillatory mechanism may be an adaptation for detecting signals from groups, while spiking for detecting from individuals. Oscillatory activity is widespread in neural networks, and may facilitate detection of signals from large groups. Behavioral experiments demonstrated that species with oscillating receptors responded strongly to submillisecond IPIs within receptor sensitivity range, while spiking receptors did not. This novel coding mechanism may represent an adaptation for communication from groups.

**Title:** Structural Insights into the Bacterophage BPP-1: Unveiling the Cryo-Electron Microscopy Structure of its Head at 3.5 Å Resolution and Implications for Phage Display Engineering**

**Research Question:** What is the three-dimensional structure of the bacteriophage BPP-1 and how does it relate to its capsid formation and phage engineering?

**Methods:** We employed single-particle cryoelectron microscopy to determine the 3D structure of BPP-1 at ∼ 3.5 Å resolution and constructed an atomic model of its head.

**Findings:** Our results reveal that BPP-1 adopts two characteristic folds of major viral lineages: jellyroll for its capsid protein (CP) and HK97-like for major capsid protein (MCP). Strikingly, the topology of MCP is non-circularly permuted from the Johnson fold previously seen in viral and cellular proteins. We show that β-sheet and electrostatic interactions contribute to non-covalent chain formation in B-1, unlike coval interprotein linkages in HK97. Despite these interactions, both CP and MCP termini are positioned for DGR-based phage engineering.

**Implications:** The atomic description of B-1 will enhance bioengineering efforts, reveal nonval molecular interactions conducive to stable capsid formation and clarify evolutionary relationships B- MCP and CP with other viruses.

**Conclusions:** This study provides the first 3D structure of B- at ∼ 3.5 Å resolution and sheds light on its capsid formation and evolutionary relationships with other viruses. The non-circular perm topology of MCP is a feasible alternative to the known HK topology, and β-sheet and electrostatic interactions contribute to nonval chain formation. These findings have implications for phage engineering and our understanding of viral evolution.

**Title:** Membrane Nanodomains Regulate Ras Signaling by Dictating KRas12 Diffusion and Trafficking in U2OS Cells

**Research Question:** How do membrane nanodomains influence the diffusion and trafficking of KRas12, an active Ras mutant, in U2 cells, and what are the implications for Ras signaling?

**Methods:** We employed single-particle tracking photoactivated localization microscopy (sPALM) and detailed trajectory analysis to investigate KRas12 diffusion and trafficking in U2 cells.

**Findings:** Our results reveal that KR12 exhibits an immobile state (~70 nm) embedded in a larger domain (~200 nm) that confers intermediate mobility, while the rest of the membrane supports fast diffusion. KR12 is continuously removed from the membrane via the immobile state and replenished to the fast, reminiscent of internalization and recycling. Importantly, diffusion and trafficking of KR12 remain invariant over a broad range of expression levels.

**Implications:** Our findings demonstrate that membrane nanodomains dictate KR12 diffusion and trafficking, offering new insights into the spatial regulation of Ras signaling. Partitioning of KR12 into three states remains invariant over a wide range of KR12 levels, indicating that diffusion and trafficking through mobility-associated nanodomains is maintained homeostatic. These data paint a picture of the spatiotemporal dynamics of KR12 on the membrane, providing a basis for understanding mechanisms of Ras multimer formation and signaling.

**Conclusions:** We propose a new model describing membrane dynamics KR12, where nanodomains dictate diffusion and trafficking, with implications for multimer and signaling. Our work sheds light on how nanodomains organize membrane to dictate Ras diffusion and trafficking, offering guidance for future experiments determining molecular and structural identities of Ras-associated nanodomains and mechanisms of multimer and signaling.

**Title:** Elucidating the Mechanism of Bone Morphogenetic Protein (BMP) Signaling Gradient Formation in the Dorsoventral Embryonic Axis of Vertebrates and Invertebrates: A Novel Insights from Zebraf Embryos

**Research Question:** How is the Boneogenetic Protein (BMP) signaling gradient formed in the dorsoventral embryonic axis of vertebrates and invertebrates, particularly in the zebraf embryo?

**Methods:** We employed a combination of quantitative imaging and mathematical modeling approaches to investigate the mechanism of BMP gradient formation in wild-type and mutant zraf embryos. We developed a robust imaging method to directly measure BMP signaling activity and integrated the results with a computational model to test hypotheses for gradient formation.

**Findings:** Contrary to the prevailing view of a counter-gradient of BMP antagonists and ligand shuttling, our analysis ruled out a shuttling and transcriptionally-formed mechanisms. We measured Bmp diffusion, which supports the source-sink model, suggesting a new mechanism to shape BMP gradients during development.

**Implications:** Our findings challenge the current understanding of BMP gradient formation in vertebrates and provide a new perspective on the role of antagonists and ligands in patterning the dorvent axis. The sourceink mechanism highlights the importance of restricted antagonist distribution in shaping the BMP gradient, underscoring the complexity of BMP systems.

**Conclusions:** Our study reveals a novel mechanism of BMP gradient formation in the zraf embryo, where a restricted antagonist acts as a sink to drive BMP dorsally gradient. The sourceink model provides a new framework for understanding BMP patterning in vertebrates and highlights the need for further investigation into the role of antagonists and ligands in shaping gradients during development.

**Title:** Visualizing Epidermal ERK Activity in Living Mice Reveals Radial Propagation of ERK Activity and Its Role in Cell Proliferation

**Research Question:** How is extracellular-regulated kinase (ER) activity regulated and propagated in the epidermis, and what is its role in cell proliferation?

**Methods:** We used transgenic mice expressing a FRET biosensor of ERK to monitor ER activity in epider cells at single-cell resolution. Long-lapse imaging of intact epider revealed radial propagation of ER activity, where ER activation originates from a few cells and propagates radially to a radius of 50 μm over 30 min. We named this phenomenon Spatial Propagation Radial ER Activity (SP) and investigated its mechanism and role in cell cycle.

**Findings:** Under steady-state conditions, the epider exhibited bursts of ER activation where ER activity radially propagated from cell to cell. The frequency of SP correlated with the rate of epider cell division. SPs were stimulated by 12-O-tetadecanoyphol-13-acet (PA) in a manner dependent on EGF receptors and their cognate ligands. At wounded skin, ER activation propagated as wave parallel to wound, suggesting ER activation can be superimposed. Visualizing cell cycle, we found SPs associated with G/M cell progression.

**Implications:** Our results provide new insights into how proliferation and ER activity are synchronized in living tissue. ER regulation is crucial for homeostasis in the epider and other tissues. ER activation is triggered by EGFR binding to cognate ligands. SPs are observed in epider and backskin, indicating generalizability of our findings.

**Conclusions:** Our study reveals radial propagation of ER activity in epider and its role in cell proliferation. SPs are associated with G/M progression, suggesting ER activity is synchronized with cell cycle. ER regulation is essential for tissue homeostasis, and our findings provide new insights into how growth signals are generated and propagated in living tissue.

**Title:** Elucidating the Principles of Circadian Rhythm Entrainment in Cyanobacteria: A Mathematical Framework for Predicting Clock Behavior in Varying Environments

**Research Question:** How do cyanobacterial circadian clocks align with external cycles, and what are the underlying principles that govern this entrainment?

**Methods:** We investigated the cyanobacterial circadian clock in both in vivo and in vitro, using purified Kai proteins to reconstitute the core oscillator. We simulated metabolic pulses of nucleotides on a 24-hr cycle, mimicking in vivo conditions, and observed linear scaling of phase with day length.

**Findings:** The phase of the circadian rhythm follows a simple scaling law in light-dark cycles, tracking midday conditions with variable length. The biochemical oscillator comprised of Kai proteins behaves similarly when driven by metabolic pulses in vitro, indicating intrinsic dynamics. We developed a mathematical framework based on instantaneous transformation of the cycle by external cues, predicting clock behavior under cycling environments.

**Implications:** The entrained behavior of the circadian clock can be captured quantitatively by a framework with a small number of parameters. This framework guides the design of experiments probing molecular origins of key features. The defined nature of the cyanobacterial clock facilitates such experiments, but the model is general, expected to be useful for other organisms as well.

**Conclusions:** Our results suggest that mechanisms for seasonal adaptation are intrinsic to the Kai proteins. The reduction in our model holds promise for predicting circadian rhythms in many-vary environments from minimal data characterizes response. **Title:** Mechanical Instabilities and Interfacial Energies Drive Biofilm Morphogenesis in Vibrio cholera Biofilms

**Research Question:** How do mechanical instabilities and interfacial energies contribute to the morphogenesis of bacterial biofilms, specifically in Vibrio cholera?

**Methods:** We employed a multi-disciplinary approach combining mechanical measurements, theory, simulation, quantitative image analysis, surface characterization, and mutagenesis to investigate the morphogenesis of Vibrio cholera biofilms.

**Findings:** Our study reveals that mechanical instabilities, including wrinkling and delamination, underlie the morphogenesis of growing biofilms. Interfacial energy is a key driver of mechanomorphogenesis, dictating the creation and annihilation of new and existing interfaces. Feedback between mechanomogenesis and bio expansion shapes the overall biofilm contour.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that mechanical forces play a crucial role in morphogenic events in biofilms, a phenomenon conserved across prokaryotes and eukaryotes. Biofilms represent a tractable model to investigate the role of mechanical forces in morphogenesis and provide a system for morph-engineering.

**Conclusions:** We propose a mechanorphogenesis model, where biofilms wrinkle and delaminate due to compressive stress (Figure 1—supplement 2). Altering the spatial distribution of mechanical stress can change bio topography. We demonstrate this by inoculating V. cholera biofilms onto agar and allowing them to collide. We speculate that Δz P aerugin mutant forms radial wrinkles and delaminations due to same mechanical instability in V. cholera. Examples illustrate how regulation of cell physiology can be coupled to mechanical instability to promote bio surface morphologies. Recent work on bio has considered mechanical instabilities. Collectively, our findings on connections between bio surface morphology and mechanical properties suggest new genes/compounds altering bio morphology by mechanics could be discovered to address bio-related problems. Morph patterns involve gene programs, but our mechanical findings in V cholera expect to be applicable to other biofilms.

**Title:** Unraveling the Mechanisms of Epileptic Neuronal Networks: Uncovering the Role of Neuron-restrictive Silencer Factor (NRS/REST) in Seizure-Induced Gene Regulation

**Research Question:** How does the neuron-restrictive silencer factor (NRS/REST) contribute to the development of epileptic neuronal networks following severe seizures?

**Methods:** We employed large-scale transcriptome analysis, quantitative PCR, chromatin immunoprecipitation (ChIP), and gene enrichment analysis (GSEA) to investigate the role of N/REST in regulating gene expression during epileptogenesis.

**Findings:** Surprisingly, a twofold to threefold increase in NREST levels, induced by seizure-provoking seizures, led to repression of only a subset of potentially regulated genes, and these were rescued by interfering with NREST function. Unexpectedly, genes selectively repressed by NREST had mid-range frequencies to the repressor, a property that rendered them sensitive to moderate fluctuations in NREST levels. Genes selectively regulated by NREST during epileogenesis coded for ion channels, receptors, and other contributors to neuronal function.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that dynamic selective regulation of NREST target genes may play a role in influencing neuronal properties in pathological and physiological contexts.

**Conclusions:**

1. NREST contributes to epileptogenesis by regulating a group of genes critically influencing neuronal function.
2. Hundreds of hippocampal genes are potential NREST targets, but only a few are actually repressed by the transcription in the context of seizure-induced epilepsy.
3. The basis of selective regulation of a subset of NREST genes includes mid-range frequencies to NREST, making them sensitive to moderate fluctuations in NREST levels.
4 NREST-regulated hippocal genes include ion channels, receptors, calcium molecules, and transcription factors governing neuronal plasticity, suggesting their repression may contribute to the pathogenesis of epilepsy.

**Title:** Unveiling the Molecular and Supramolecular Determinants of Allosteric Effects in the M2 Muscarinic Receptor: A Comparative Study of Monomers and Oligomers

**Research Question:** How do monomers and oligomers of the M2 muscarinic receptor exhibit allosteric properties, and what are the molecular and supramolecular determinants of these effects?

**Methods:** We employed a FRET-based sensor to monitor alloster conformation at the alloster site and pharmacological assays involving engineered mutants to exclude intramolecular effects. Electrostatic, steric, and conformational determinants of alloster at the atomic level were examined using molecular dynamics simulations.

**Findings:** Alloic effects in monomers were exclusively negative and primarily derived from intramolecular electrostatic repulsion between alloster and orthost ligands. Allo effects oligomers could be positive or negative, depending on alloster-orth pair, arising from interactions between constituent protomers. The behavior of oligomers is characteristic of muscarinic receptors in myocardial preparations.

**Implications:** Our findings challenge the traditional view that allosteric effects in the M2 muscar receptor are solely due to interactions within a monomer, highlighting the importance of oligomeric nature of GPCRs. Alloic effects in oligomers arise from interactions between constituent protomers, not solely from alloster site modulation of orthost site.

**Conclusions:** This study provides new insights into the molecular and supramolecular determinants of alloic effects in the M muscarinic receptor, a prototypic model of allosteric GPCRs. Our findings have implications for understanding the complex behavior of muscarinic receptors in myocardial preparations and the broader family of GPCRs.

Loss of SET2 in CC may provide an alternative mechanism for inactivation of p53 without additional mutations in TP53. This study provides a link between altered chromatin states and mechanisms maintaining genome integrity. Further studies are needed to elucidate the mechanistic basis of SET2 impact on the DNA damage response.

**Title:** Sex-Specific Effects of Social Isolation on the Properties of Corticotropin-Releasing Hormone (CRH) Neurons in the Paraventricular Nucleus (PVN) of the Hypothalamus

**Research Question:** How does social isolation affect the intrinsic properties of corticotropin-releasing hormone (CRH) neurons in the paraventricular nucleus (PV) of the hypothalamus in male and female mice, and how do these effects compare to those of acute physical stress?

**Methods:** We investigated the effects of social isolation and acute stress on the intrinsic properties of CRH neurons in the PV of the hypothalamus in male and female mice. Pre-adolescent female mice were isolated from littermates for 24 hours, while age-matched males were not. We also subjected either isolated or group-housed females and males to acute physical stress (swim) for 20 minutes. Intrinsic properties of CRH neurons were assessed in hypothalamic brain slices.

**Findings:** Social isolation increased first latency (FSL) and decreased excitability of CR neurons in socially-isolated female, but not age-matched male mice. Acute stress increased FSL in groupoused females and singleoused males. The increase in F following social or acute stress was blocked by glucocortic synthesis inhibitor metrapone and mimicked by exogenous corticosterone. The increase in F resulted in decrease in excitability of CR neurons.

**Implications:** Our findings demonstrate that social, but not acute stress, has sex-specific effects on PV CRH neurons. Social isolation alters intrinsic properties of CRH neurons in a sexually dimorphic fashion, increasing F and decreasing excitability in socially-isolated female, but not ageatched male mice. Acute stress, however, has indistinguishable effects on males and females.

**Conclusions:** Our observations suggest that social isolation, but not acute stress, has sex-specific effects on PV CRH neurons. Social isolation may be interpreted as stress by female, but not male mice, as evidenced by prior isolation occluding effects of acute stress on F in females but not males.

However, results from the dataset support that changes in mammalian H binding and conformation have been tuned, giving rise to maximum cooper in binding, albeit different n in each case. The human dataset shows a positive correlation between metabolic rate and maximum cooperativity, and an inverse correlation between rate and affinity.

**Title:** Neural Mechanisms of Economic Decisions: Unveiling the Role of the Medial Prefrontal Cortex in Commitment and Rejection

**Research Question:** How do humans make economic decisions involving commitment and rejection, and what neural mechanisms underlie these processes in the medial prefrontal cortex?

**Methods:** In this study, we investigated the neural mechanisms of economic decisions involving commitment and rejection using a multi-altern choice task with financial consequences that persisted over time. Participants made successive choices between accepting or deferring a prospect, with the option to commit or reject later. We used functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to examine brain activity in the medial prefrontal cortex (mFC) and dorsal anterior cingulate (ACC) cortices.

**Findings:** We found that the rostral medial prefrontal cortex (rmFC) encoded value only when acceptance or rejection was deferred into the future, suggesting a role in integrating signals over time. In contrast, the dorsal anterior cing cortex (ACC) encoded value only when participants rejected or accepted a prospect. ACC signals reflected two biases: deferring to later and weighting potential losses more heavily than gains, paradoxically maximizing reward in the task.

**Implications:** These findings provide new insights into the pressures that shape economic decisions and computation in the prefrontal cortex. Our results suggest that value encoding in the medialfront cortex depends on whether decisions involve commitment or not, and that the dorsal cing cortex plays a critical role in weighing losses and gains.

**Conclusions:** This study reveals the neural mechanisms of economic decisions involving commitment and rejection, and highlights the importance of the prefrontal cortex in integrating value signals over time. Our findings have implications for understanding the neural basis of economic behavior and decision-making.

**Title:** Investigating the Dynamics of Spliceosome Assembly in Human Pre-mRNA Splicing: Unveiling the Role of Cross-in and Crosson Interactions

**Research Question:** How do cross-in and crosson interactions contribute to the assembly of the spliceosome in human pre-mRNA splicing, and what are the molecular mechanisms underlying this process?

**Methods:** We employed a novel approach using fluorescently tagged spliceosomal subcomplexes in human nuclear extracts to investigate the dynamics of pre-mRNA interactions with individual spliceosomal subcomplexes, focusing on the role of cross-in and cross interactions in promoting pre-splice assembly.

**Findings:** Our results show that U1 binding to the 5' splice site of an intron, in conjunction with U1 bound to the 5' site of the adjacent intron, significantly increases the rate and efficiency of U2 recruitment to the 3' splice site of the upstream intron. Notably, flanking 5 splice sites exhibit additive effects, suggesting distinct mechanisms facilitating U2 recruitment. This synergy of 5 splice sites across introns and exons is crucial for the correct and efficient splicing of multi-in preRNAs.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest an ordered pre-splice assembly pathway in human, where U2 association requires prior U1 binding in the presence of ATP, contrasting the branched pathway observed in S. cerevisiae (Shbak et al., 2013). In multi-in preRNAs, internal exons 5'S both upstream and downstream, or both, can bind U and act to recruit U to 3'S. In principle, two U1 can act independently or synergistically.

**Conclusions:** We establish methods for observing spliceosomal subcomplex dynamics on preRNA splicing in human extracts, revealing that U binding to 5'S is unaffected by U sites positioned downstream (crosson) or upstream (cross) of 5'S. Spliceosomes can form on multi-in preRNAs through two pathways: a) cross-in interactions leading to catalytically active spliceosome, or b) crosson interactions where exons flanking introns are defined, followed by cross-in interactions between adjacent exons leading to spliceosome (Mold and Query, 2010). Our models propose that once a functional U-U prelice forms across intr, interactions with U bound downstream 5 provide no additional U binding or stabilization of subsequent steps.

**Title:** Unveiling a Novel Ubiquitin-Dependent Folding System for Membrane Proteins on the Cytosolic Side of the Endoplasmic Reticulum (ER)

**Findings:** We discovered a novel ubiquitin-dependent folding system that operates on the cytosolic side of the ER, involving site-specific ubiquitination and deubiquination cycles. **Implications:** This ubiquitin-dependent folding system controls proteostasis of membrane proteins involved in severe human diseases, such as cystic fibrosis and Hyine Fibrosis Syndrome.

**Conclusions:** Our findings reveal a novel ubiquitin-dependent folding machinery on the cytos side of ER, dependent on site-specific cycles of ubiquit-deubiquination. This system likely operates on other membrane proteins, such as CFTR and anthrax receptor 2. In analogy to glucylation-deglucylation in the lumen, we hypothesize lys-140-ubitin interacts with a putative ubiqu-binding protein acting as a chaper. Release from this ubiqu-binding chaper, L6 is deubiquinated by US19. Our study highlights the importance of ubiquitin-dependent folding in membrane protein quality control and its potential therapeutic implications for human diseases.

**Title:** Sex-Based Modulation of Cognitive Processes Sets the Stage for Individual Differences in Vulnerability to Neuropsychiatric Disorders: Uncovering Latent Variables in Value-Based Decision Making

**Research Question:** Do sex-based differences in cognitive processes, particularly in value-based decision making, contribute to individual vulnerability to neuropsychiatric disorders?

**Findings:** Male mice made more exploratory choices, getting stuck in exploration once started, while females explored less but learned more quickly. **Implications:** Sex differences in exploration contribute to performance variability across tasks and species (Grom and Reyes, 2019; van Bos et al, 2013). We applied computational tools to characterize sex differences in latent variables underlying behavior, understanding key process regulating reward-guided behaviors: exploration-exploitation.

**Results:** Hidden Markov Model (HMM) and Reinforcement Learning (RL) models revealed sex differences in exploration, paralleled by differences in learning rate. How exploration-exploitation, reward, and outcomes interact to influence choices remains unclear.

**Conclusions:** Mechanisms biasing approaches during tasks are significant contributors to performance. We used combination modeling to characterize differences in explore-exploit task. Males and females had similar performance, but used different latent strategies to learn environment. Post-hoc win-lose analyses showed females displayed more win-st behaviors, indicating more likely to repeat behaviors producing reward on previous trial.

**Title:** Deciphering the Mechanisms of Hedgehog Signaling in Drosophila Wing Imaginal Discs: Unraveling the Roles of Ci Processing and Regulation

**Research Question:** How do extracellular Hedgehog (H) proteins regulate transcriptional changes in target cells by inhibiting the proteolytic processing of full-length Drosoph Ci or mammalian Gli proteins to transcriptional repressors and activating the full Ci Gli proteins, and what are the contributions of these mechanisms to dose-dependent H signaling in Dophila wing imaginal discs?

**Methods:** We investigated the contributions of Ci variants expressed at physiological levels to the mechanisms of H signaling in Doph wing imaginal discs. **Conclusions:** Our study unravels the mechanisms of H signaling in D wing imaginal discs, highlighting the contributions of Ci processing and regulation to dose-dependent H signaling. We demonstrate that Ci can mediate normal H morph action in the absence regulated processing, and that processing-resistant Ci variants support development with normal wing pattering provided constitutive repressor is present. These findings have implications for understanding H signaling in development and disease, where aberrant H production, distribution, and responses are common causes of developmental defects and cancer.

Future work must determine how these circuits respond to peripherally-evoked sensory processing. Recent studies have implicated neurochemically-distinct subpopulations of dorsal interneurons in both acute and chronic pain states, while historically, research has focused on inhibition. Our data is compatible with current models placing CR in polysynaptic circuits through SOM to activate lamina projection and initiate pain.

**Title:** Structural Insights into the Coat Protein-RNA Interactions of Pepino Mosaic Virus, a Representative of the Potexivirus Family

**Research Question:** What is the three-dimensional structure of the coat protein (CP) of Pepino mosaic virus (PepMV), a representative of the Potexivirus family, and how does it interact with its viral RNA?

**Methods:** High-resolution cryo-electron microscopy was used to determine the structure of PepMV CP at 3.9 Å resolution, allowing for modeling of the protein and its interactions with viral RNA.

**Findings:** The overall fold of PepMV CP resembles that of nucleoproteins (NPs) from the Phlebovirus family, a group of enveloped ssRNA viruses, differing in C-terminal extensions that determine distinct multimer assemblies. Surprisingly, a structural homology was found between CP of flexuous viruses and NPs of Phleviruses, a group of enveloped ssRNA viruses.

**Implications:** This study provides insights into the structure-function relationships between CP and RNA of flexuous viruses and enveloped viruses, suggesting gene transfer between eukaryotic and ssRNA viruses.

**Conclusions:** The structure of PepMV CP, a representative of the Potex family, was determined at 3.9 Å resolution by cryo-electron microscopy, revealing a fold similar to that of NPs from Phlevir, a group of enveloped ssRNA viruses, differing in C-terminal extensions that determine distinct multimer assemblies. Homology was found between CP of flexuous viruses and NPs of Phvir, a group of enveloped ssRNA viruses, suggesting gene transfer between eukotic and ssRNA viruses.

**Title:** High-Resolution Imaging of Antibody Binding Sites on Heterogeneous Cellular Surfaces using SiteLoc: A Novel Method for Epitope Localization

**Findings:** Our results demonstrate that Site is well-suited for antibody binding measurements on native surface morphology and can be applied to other molecular estimations. We achieved nan-scale precision by reducing surface data dimension within an image plane. **Implications:** Fast and efficient epitope screening is crucial for therapeutic development. Our method addresses this limitation, enabling high-throughput analysis of binding sites on cellular surfaces.

**Conclusions:** Site offers a novel solution for high-resolution imaging of antibody binding on heterogeneous surfaces. This method has potential for mechanistic understanding and therapeutic development of monoclonal antibodies and vaccines. Our results show that Site can be applied to other molecular estimations, such as protein-protein interactions, with nan-scale precision. Future work will explore applications of this method in various biological systems and therapeutic contexts.

**Conclusions:** This study provides the first demonstration of a dynamic role for oxygen sensing in peripheral tissue modifying cardiovascular response to hypia. Our results underscore the significance of peripheral sensing in systemic vascular regulation, particularly in response to hypoxia. These findings have implications for understanding cardiovascular adaptation to hypia in various conditions, such as high altitude or disease.

**Title:** Unraveling the Complexity of Transcription Factor Interactions on Eukaryotic Genomes: A Biophysical Analysis of Sox2 and Oct4 Reciprocity

**Research Question:** How do transcription factors (TFs) Sox2 and Oct4 interact with eukaryotic genomes, and what is the nature of their binding dynamics?

**Methods:** We conducted a rigorous biophysical analysis of single-molecule data from living cells to investigate the interaction between Sox2 and Oct4, previously interpreted as evidence of ordered assembly on DNA.

**Findings:** Contrary to previous findings, our analysis reveals that Sox2 and Oct exhibit reciprocity, with Sox2 increasing Oct4's genomic binding while decreasing Oct4 Sox's binding. This reciprocity can arise from either energy expenditure from thermodynamic equilibrium or a mixture of positive and negative cooperativity at distinct genomic loci.

**Implications:** Our study highlights unexpected complexity in TF interactions on DNA, underscoring the need for single-molecule methods to detect novel mechanisms. The genomic-divers model suggests that Sox and Oct sometimes help, sometimes hinder each other across the genome, challenging previous assumptions of ordered assembly. This finding has significant ramifications for interpreting single-m data and understanding TF interactions across the genome.

**Conclusions:** Our analysis reveals tantalizing possibility of energy expenditure in Sox and Oct's action on DNA, previously unexplored in living cells. Single-molecule methods offer exciting capabilities to unravel such mechanisms. The variation in cooperativity across genome suggests that single-m data, coupled with experimental strategy, provides more information than expected. Notably, a single number can indicate both positive and negative cooperativity across loci in vivo. The genomic-divers model implies either energy expenditure or mixed cooperativity, with Sox and Oct helping each other at some loci and hindering at others. This study underscores the need for further investigation into TF interactions across the genome to properly interpret single-m data.

* Zfp is essential for naïve T cell maturation
* Zfp regulates transcription by binding to promoters of target genes

**Title:** Elucidating the Role of mRNA:ncRNA Interactions in Protein Abundance Regulation: A Novel Insights into the Mechanisms of Translational Efficiency

**Research Question:** Do mRNA:RNA interactions significantly impact protein abundance, and if so, what are the underlying mechanisms driving this relationship?

**Methods:** We employed a multi-faceted approach to investigate the relationship between mRNA:RNA interactions and protein abundance. Firstly, we generated synonymous variant green fluorescent protein (GFP) mRNAs with varying potential for mRNA:RNA interactions and demonstrated a strong correlation between GFP levels and avoidance. Next, we analyzed 97 species and found a reduced capacity for mRNA:RNA interactions relative to controls. Furthermore, we generated variant mRNAs that differ in their potential to interact with non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs) and showed that GFP levels can be predicted and controlled.

**Findings:** Our results demonstrate a strong selection for avoidance of stochastic mRNA:RNA interactions across prokaryotes, which has a greater impact on protein abundance than mRNA structure or codon usage. Moreover, measuring extrinsic avoidance using shuffled mRN as controls revealed that mRNA:RNA interactions are significantly underrepresented in 73% of proary phyla (p<0.05). These findings establish a signature of selection acting to minimize mRNA:RNA interactions. However, with thousands of potential interacting RNAs in simple proary systems, complete avoidance is combinatorially unlikely, suggesting a trade-off between avoidance and optimal expression.

**Implications:** Our results indicate that mRNA:RNA interactions impact protein levels, and taking them into account will enable precise protein expression control. We predict that mRNAs with low avoidance will produce fewer proteins per transcript than those with high avoidance. To test this, we conducted a meta-analysis of proteomics and transcriptomics data and found a significant correlation between mRNA:RNA measures and protein levels.

**Conclusions:** This study reveals a novel mechanism of translational efficiency, mRNAncRNA avoidance, and demonstrates its impact on protein abundance. Our findings have significant implications for the design of constructs where protein levels can be precisely controlled. Furthermore, they highlight the importance of considering mRNA:RNA interactions in understanding protein regulation.

**Title:** Unraveling the Complexity of Neuromuscular Junction Denervation in Amyotrophic Lateral Sclerosis: A Longitudinal In Vivo Imaging Reveals Asynchronous Motor Unit Dismantling and New Synapse Formation**

**Research Question:** Does neuromuscular junction (NM) denervation in amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS) result from global motor neuron degeneration or local branch-specific events?

**Methods:** We employed longitudinal in vivo imaging of single motor axons and their neuromuscular junctions (NMJs) in SOD1G37 mice over a three-month period to investigate the dynamics of NM denervation in ALS.

**Findings:** Our results reveal that motor units are dismantled asynchronously, with axonal branches being dismantled prior to complete degeneration, accompanied by contemporaneous axonal sprouting and synapse formation onto nearby NMJs. Denervation events propagate from the first NM, consistent with a contribution of neuromuscular factors extrinsic to motor neurons, with distal branches being more susceptible.

**Implications:** These findings indicate that NM denervation in ALS is a complex process of continuous denervation and new innervation rather than a manifestation of sudden motor degeneration. A temporal window exists between branch-specific dismantling and global axonal degeneration, which could be therapeutically exploited.

**Conclusions:** Our longitudinal in vivo analysis reveals a previously unsuspected dynamism at the NM in an animal model of ALS, highlighting a long window between early branch-specific dismantling and motor degeneration. Local-specific events may play a key role in NM denervation, as rescue of neuronal loss in S1 mice does not necessarily improve NM denervation. Alterations in synaptic function and molecular signaling occur early at NM in ALS, suggesting a complex interplay of local and global mechanisms in disease progression.

**Title:** DeepRET: An Automated Single-Molecule Förster Resonance Energy Transfer Analysis using Deep Learning for Biomolecular Dynamics

**Research Question:** Can a deep learning-based approach be developed to automate the analysis of single-molecule Försteron resonance energy transfer (smRET) data, enabling rapid and objective quantification of biomolecular dynamics?

**Methods:** We present DeepRET, an open-source, standalone software that utilizes deep learning to analyze smRET data with minimal human intervention, requiring only a user-adjustable threshold for image quality. Integrating standard smRET features, DeepRET outputs kinetic metrics, outperforming human operators with >95% accuracy and requiring ~1% of the time.

**Findings:** DeepRET achieved high accuracy on ground truth data, demonstrating its capacity to objectively quantify biomolecular dynamics and contribute to benchmarking smRET for structural biology. Training on simulated or high-quality data, we expect neural networks like DeepRET to enable fully automated methodologies for biomolecular recognition, folding, and resolution.

**Implications:** DeepRET, with a pre-trained DNN core, offers rapid, objective, and accurate assessment of smRET for quantifying biomolecular dynamics. The fully automated software operates with minimal human intervention, requiring only a threshold on data quality, outputting detailed quantification of structural from raw images. DeepRET comes as a free, executable for Windows and Mac users, allowing end-users with limited skills to operate.

**Conclusions:** Deep-based analysis outperforms other approaches by recognizing patterns and learning features from raw data, allowing implementation of routines without extensive preprocessing or empirically defined rules, and reproducible evaluation of single-molecule data. It is faster than human for large datasets, coming close to or improved classification without preprocessing or post-inspection data, illustrating DeepRET's power to operate without interventions and benchmark reproducibility of smRET methods for multiple systems.

**Title:** SCML2, a Polycomb Group Protein, Regulates Epigenetic Gene Expression through RNA Binding and Interaction with Polycomb Repressive Complex 1 (PRC1)

Our data show RNA plays a role in SCML function, required for localization to chromatin. In Dophila, interaction SCM and PR relies on C-terminal SPM (Peterson et al., 2004), our results show conserved in humans. We propose a model where BR contributes localization SCML to chrom, interacting PR, creating chrom environment refractory to transcription.

**Title:** Elucidating the Mechanisms of Sec-Mediated Translational Stalling in Escherichia coli Ribosomes through Cryo-EM Structures

**Findings:** Our cryo-EM structures of Sec-stalled ribosomes at 3.3-3.7 resolution reveal two stalling mechanisms: (1) inactivation of the ribosomal peptid-transfer center inhibiting peptide bond formation with incoming tRNA, and (2) prolonged pept-RNA residence at the A site inhibiting full tRNA translocation. Together with previous structural studies of ribosomes stalled by antibiotics, our work reveals diverse mechanisms of translational control.

**Conclusions:** Our cryo-EM structures of Sec-st ribosomes reveal two distinct stalling mechanisms at elongation steps, providing insights into the molecular basis of Sec-dependent translational stalling in Escheria ribosomes. These findings have implications for understanding translational control and regulation in bacteria, highlighting the dynamic reshaping of the ribosomal center by regulatory peptides.

**Title:** Foveal Cone Density as a Function of Axial Length in Eyes: Physical and Visual Implications

**Research Question:** This study investigates the relationship between foveal cone density and axial length in eyes, aiming to elucidate the structural changes occurring at the fovea center during myopic progression.

**Methods:** We employed an Adaptive Optics Scanning Ophthalmoscope to image cones at the fove in 28 eyes of 16 subjects. Cone density and metrics were computed in units of visual angle and linear retinal units.

**Findings:** Contrary to expectations, the fove cone mosaic in longer eyes is expanded at the fove, but not proportionally to length. Despite retinal stretching (cones/mm2), myopes exhibit higher angular density (cones/deg) around the fove compared to emmetropes, potentially offering better visual acuity. Reports of best-corrected fove vision deficits in myopes compared to emrop cannot be explained by increased spacing photoreceptors caused by retinal stretching during my progression.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that myopes have higher fove photore sampling, enabling better performance on visual tasks compared to emrop. This contradicts previous studies implicating retinal stretching as a cause for myopic deficits. Instead, our results indicate that the fove cone mosaic adapts to compensate for axial elongation, preserving visual acuity.

**Conclusions:** This study provides definitive analysis of cone density at the fove center as a function of eye length, resolving a long-standing question in the field. Our findings have significant implications for understanding myopic vision and visual acuity, highlighting the importance of fove adaptation in maintaining visual performance.

**Title:** Elucidating the Genetic Determinants of Streptococcus pneumoniae Carriage Duration: A Genome-Wide Analysis of Longitudinal Swab Data

**Research Question:** We aimed to develop a model to calculate the duration of carriage episodes from longitudinal swab data and combine these with whole genome data to estimate the genetic contribution of pneumoccal variation to carriage duration.

**Methods:** We analyzed longitudinal swab data from a cohort of infants and combined with whole genome data to estimate heritability of carriage duration. We partitioned herit into lineage and locus effects and quantified the attributable sources of variation.

**Findings:** Pneumoccal genomic variation accounted for 63% of the phenotype, whereas host traits (age and previous carriage) accounted for less than 5%. We identified the largest sources of variation: serotype (17%), drug-resistance (9%), and other locus (7%). **Implications:** These findings support theoretical models of pneumoccal competition and antibiotic resistance. We identified candidates for loci fulfilling this role, suggesting pneumoc genome variants alter carriage, linked to antibiotic resistance.

**Conclusions:** The pneumoc genome explains most variability in carriage duration, with serotype and erythromycin resistance causing 19% and prophage presence/absence 7%. This has corollaries on pneumoc genome diversification through frequent infection loss of prophage during episodes. The genetics of S. pneumonia is a factor in determining duration in this population. Environmental conditions, such as host status, may alter herit estimate.

**Title:** Investigating the Impact of Protein Properties and Ionic Strength on Translational Diffusion in the Cytoplasm of Prokaryotes

**Research Question:** How do protein surface properties and ionic strength influence the translational diffusion of proteins in the cytoplasm of proaryotes, and what are the implications for the composition of the cytoplasm proteome?

**Methods:** We employed surface-modified fluorescent proteins (FPs) to measure translational coefficients (D) in the cytoplasm of Escheria coli, Lactoccus lactis, and Haloaxii. We analyzed the dependence of D on protein net charge and charge distribution, as well as the effect of electrostatic screening and ribosome interactions.

**Findings:** In E. coli, D depends on net charge and distribution, with positive proteins diffusing up to 100-fold slower than negative ones. This effect is weaker in L. lact and H. due to electrostatic screening. Decrease in mobility is caused by interaction of positive FPs with ribosomes, confirmed in vivo and in vitro with purified ribosomes. Ribosome properties may limit cytoplasmic proteome composition.

**Implications:** Our findings reveal a paradox in proary endosymbiotic functioning. In L. lact and H, slowdown with increasing positive charge is less pronounced due to electrostatic screening. Decrease in D is mainly caused by binding of positive to ribosomes, with KD on order µM, indicating non-selective interactions. Ribosome properties may limit cytoplasm proteome composition.

**Conclusions:** Transl diffusion of proteins in E. coli depends on net charge and distribution, with positive proteins diffusing up to 100-fold slower than negative. This effect is weaker in L. and H due to electrostatic screening. Decrease in mobility is caused by interaction of positive with ribosomes. Ribosome properties may limit cytoplasmic proteome composition. These findings have implications for understanding proary endosymbiotic functioning and the role of ribosomes in protein synthesis.

**Research Question:** What are the signaling pathways triggered by ribosome stalling during neurodegeneration, and how do they impact neuronal survival?

**Methods:** We investigated the brain of C57/BL/6-Gbp2f2-/- mice, where elongation complexes are stalled at AG codons due to deficiencies in tRNA and Gbp2, a mammalian ribosome rescue factor.

**Findings:**

* Transcriptome analysis demonstrated activation of ATF, a key transcription factor in the integrated stress response (ISR) pathway
* Genetic experiments showed this pathway was activated by e2 kinase, GC2, in a deacylated tRNA-independent fashion
* ISR attenuated neurogeneration in C57/BL/6-Gbp2-/- mice, underscoring the importance of cellular context on the outcome of this pathway

**Implications:**

* Ribosome stalling activates GC2, a previously unknown mechanism linking elongation and initiation of translation
* Failure to resolve elong complexes in mammalian neurons results in degeneration, but pathways triggered by these complexes were unknown
* GC2 activates ISR in the brain of mice with stalled ribosomes, demonstrating a feedback loop between elongation defects and initiation
* ATF activation is vital for survival of neurons during ribosome stalling

**Conclusions:**

The integrated stress response pathway is activated by GC2 in response to ribosome stalling, which attenuates neurogeneration in C57/BL/6-Gbp2/- mice. These findings highlight the importance of cellular context on the outcome of this pathway and suggest ribosome stalling may contribute to GC2 activation in other conditions, such as amino acid deprivation. **Title:** Uneven Global Changes in Adult Height Over the 20th Century: Implications for Longevity and Socioeconomic Outcomes**

**Research Question:** How has adult height changed across the world over the past century, and what are the implications for longevity and socioeconomic outcomes?

**Methods:** We analyzed 147 population-based studies with measurements of over 18.6 million participants, estimating height for individuals born between 1896 and 1996 in 200 countries.

**Findings:** The largest gains in adult height occurred in South Korean women and Iranian men, who became 20.2 cm (95% CI 17.5-22.7) and 16.5 cm (13.3-19.7) taller, respectively. In contrast, little change in adult height was observed in sub-Saharan Africa and South Asia. The tallest individuals were men born in the Netherlands in the last quarter of the 20th century, averaging 182.5 cm, while the shortest were women born in Guatemala (140.3; 135.8-144.8). The height differential between the tallest and shortest populations 100 years ago remained the same for women and increased for men, despite changes in country rankings.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that adult height has changed unevenly across the world, with no indication of convergence. The height differential between the tallest and shortest was 19 cm for men and 20 for women 100 years ago, and remained the same for women and increased for men, despite changes in country rankings. The largest gains since the 1896 birth cohort occurred in South Korean and Iranian men, who became 20.2 (17.5-22.7) and 16.5 (13.3-19.3) taller than women in Guatemala. Women were taller than 158 in Norway, Iceland, USA, and Samoa. The tallest populations 100 years ago lived in Central Europe, North America, and Pacific islands. Men in Sweden, Norway, and USA surpassed 171, 18 taller than in Laos. Men in 199 surpassed 181 in Netherlands, Belgium, Estonia, Latvia, and Denmark, with Dutch at 182.5 (180.6-184.5), the tallest on the planet. The gap with shortest countries Timor, Yemen, and Laos, men only 160 tall is 22, an increase 4 on the global in 189 birth.

**Conclusion:** Our study highlights the substantial and uneven changes in adult height across the world, with implications for longevity and socioeconomic outcomes. The largest gains in height occurred in South Korean and Iranian men, while little change was observed in sub-Saharan Africa and Asia. The tallest individuals were born in Netherlands, while shortest in Guatemala. The differential between tallest and shortest populations remained the same for women and increased for men, despite changes in country rankings. **Conclusions:** Our study identifies NT as the first confirmed functional receptor for HBV and HD, a major public health problem worldwide. **Conclusions:** APOEC3 cytidine deamin is a potent inhibitor of LINE-1 retroposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA during the process of integration. This mechanism explains why overexpression of wild-type A3, but not deamin-deficient A3 mutants, potently inhibits L retro in cultured cells. Our findings have significant implications for understanding the regulation of LINE-1 retroposition in human cells and the role of A in genomic stability.

**Title:** Reevaluating the Claims of Magnetism in Biological Systems: A Critical Analysis of the Proposed Mechanisms and Implications

**Research Question:** Can magnetic fields indeed influence biological molecules and cells as proposed by recent studies, and if so, what are the underlying mechanisms and implications?

**Methods:** A critical analysis of existing literature on magnetism in biological systems, focusing on reports of protein complexes acting as magnetic compasses (Qin et al., 2016), magnetic control of membrane conductance by ferritin (Stanley et al., 201; Wheeler et al., 2016), and magnetogenetic systems (201).

**Findings:** The proposed mechanisms of magnetism in biological systems conflict with basic laws of physics, exhibiting discrepancies of 5-10 orders of magnitude. The paramagnetic nature of protein complexes limits their utility for magnetically sensitive cells. Forces and torques produced are too small to affect molecular orientation or membrane channels.

**Implications:** If the phenomena occur, they must rely on different mechanisms. Barring new discoveries on biological matter structure, magnetogen routes based on pulling ferritin/channel with magnetic fields have no chance of success.

**Conclusions:**

Recent studies on magnetism in biological systems have sparked interest in magnetogenetics, the dream of controlling cells with magnetic fields. However, a critical analysis reveals discrepancies between proposed mechanisms and laws of physics. The paramagnetic nature of protein complexes limits their utility for magnetically sensitive cells. Forces and torques produced are too small to affect molecular orientation or membrane channels. If the phenomena occur, they must rely on different mechanisms. Barring new discoveries on biological structure, magnetogen routes have no chance of success.

**Title:** Unveiling the Role of Prospero in Regulating Stem Cell Quies in Drosophila Neuroblasts

**Research Question:** How do stem cells switch between self-renewal, differentiation, and quiescence, and what are the molecular mechanisms underlying this transition?

**Background:** Drosila neuroblasts have been an excellent model for studying self-renew and differentiation, but quies remains poorly understood.

**Methods:** We investigated the role of Prosper, a transcription factor, in regulating quies in Drosila neuroblasts.

**Findings:** We show that Prosper is transiently detected in the neuroblast nucleus prior to entry into quies, followed by establishment of a unique molecular profile lacking progenitor and differentiation markers. Low levels of Prosper precede entry into quies, even when timing is advanced or delayed by temporal factors. Loss of Prosper prevents quies, whereas low levels drive proliferating larval neuroblasts into quies.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that Prosper levels distinguish three progenitor states: absent self-renew, low quies, and high differentiation.

**Conclusions:** We propose that Prosper is a key regulator of quies in Dila neuroblasts, with transient low levels marking entry into quies. Our results have implications for stem cell therapeutics, as understanding how stem cells switch between states is crucial for regenerative medicine.

**Key Findings:**

* Prosper is transiently detected in neuroblast nucleus prior to quies
* Loss of Prosper prevents quies
* Low levels drive proliferating larval neurobl into quies

**Title:** Unraveling the Mysteries of HIV-1 Capsid Disassembly: A Single-Molecule Fluorescence Microscopy Reveals Kinetics and Regulation

**Research Question:** How is the metastable HIV-1 capsid disassembly process regulated, and how can it be modulated, to facilitate the release of viral DNA prior to nuclear import?

**Methods:** We developed a single-molecule fluorescence microscopy method to track real-time unco kinetics of authentic HIV-1 capsids in vitro after permeabilizing the viral membrane.

**Findings:**

1. Opening the first defect in the lattice is the rate-limiting step of unco, followed by rapid, catastrophic collapse.
2. Caps-binding PF74 accelerates caps opening but stabilizes the lattice.
3. Binding of polyan to a conserved arginine cluster in lattice delays initiation of unco but does not prevent lattice disassembly.

**Implications:** Different stages of unco can be controlled by the interplay between capsid regulators, determining overall unco kinetics.

**Conclusions:**

Our single-molecule fluorescence microscopy reveals that HIV-1 caps disassembly is a tightly regulated process, with distinct stages controlled by interplay between capsid regulators. Caps-binding molecules exhibit differential effects on initiation and continuation of unco. The structural heterogeneity of HIV-1 caps is described, with assembly yielding conical, tubular, and other shapes containing lattice defects. Our assay resolves intermediates in disassembly averaged in traditional ensemble measurements, allowing classification of individual caps according to unating behaviors.

**Key Findings:**

1. Capsid integrity and stability is heterogeneous, with majority containing defects and uncoating immediately after membrane removal.
2 In absence of binders, unco is all-or process proceeding rapidly after first defect created.
3 Half-life of caps is sufficient for P-permeabilized particles as platform to quantify binding labeled proteins to authentic caps at single level.

**Implications:**

Our findings provide new insights into HIV-1 caps disassembly and regulation, highlighting potential targets for therapeutic intervention. Caps-binding molecules can modulate unco kinetics, with PF74 accelerating opening and stabilizing lattice, while polyan delaying initiation but not preventing disassembly. These results suggest distinct stages of unco can be controlled by interplay between capsid regulators, determining overall kinetics.

**Research Question:** How do voltage-gated calcium channels (CaVs) achieve their diverse functions in excitable cells, such as synaptic transmission in neurons and contraction of heart and skeletal muscles, by activating at different voltages and kinetics? Specifically, what are the molecular mechanisms governing voltage sensing in the two functionally distinct voltage-sensing domains (VSDs) of the eukotic calcium channel Ca1.1?

This mechanism provides a mechanistic explanation for how ionair formation in VSDs realize characteristic properties of voltageated cation channels.

**Implications:** Our study fills an important gap in understanding voltage sensing in eotic voltageated cation channels. Tested interactions predicted by the model showed effects on gating when experimentally tested by mutesis and electrophys analysis. Alterations of kinetic properties recognized experimentally were reliably explained by our model. Structures of lowest energy states Ca1.1 VSD display basic features of sliding helix model (atter et al 2017; Yarovarovoy et al 2012).

**Conclusions:** This study provides a mechanistic model for specific ionair in VSD to realize gating properties of voltageated cation channels. Our findings highlight the importance of ionair formation in VSD in voltage sensing and provide a framework for understanding the diverse functions of voltageated channels in excitable cells.

**Title:** The Role of Matrix Metalloproteinases in Adult Visual Cortex Plasticity: Unveiling the Optimal Regulation for Recovery and Learning**

**Research Question:** Do matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) play a crucial role in adult visual cortex plasticity, and can inhibition of their upregulation after stroke rescue lost plasticity?

**Methods:** We investigated the effects of MMP inhibition on experience-dependent plasticity in healthy and stroke-affected adult mice using a combination of in vivo imaging and behavioral tests.

**Findings:** In healthy animals, 7-day application of MMP-inhib prevented plasticity, while treatment with MMP-inhib once, but not twice, rescued plasticity normally lost under stroke conditions. Our data suggest that an optimal level of MMPactivity is crucial for adult plasticity to occur.

**Implications:** These findings highlight a critical role of MMPs in adult plasticity and suggest that upregulation of MMPactivity shortly after cortical lesion compromises experience-induced plastic, which can be rescued by brief MMP-inhibition. Precise regulation of MMPactivity is essential for plasticity in the adult brain, offering new opportunities for treatment and recovery after stroke.

**Conclusion:** Our study demonstrates a vital role of MMP for visual cortex plastic, particularly in the increase of open responses V1 after MD, expanding previous studies from juvenile rats. Under normal conditions, MMPactivity supports brain development and function; however, different MMPaction has been described in diseased brain. Increased MMPactivity was observed 24 hours after photothromically-induced stroke, and application of broad MMP-inhib at time stroke partially rescued barrel plasticity. We tested if stroke-induced impairment of cortical plastic can be rescued by inhibiting MMP after stroke.

**Key Findings:**

* Optimal MMPactivity is essential for adult plasticity to occur in healthy and stroke-affected brain
* Upregulation of MMP shortly after cortical lesion compromises experience plastic, which can be rescued by MMP-in
Precise regulation MMPactivity is crucial for plastic in adult brain
Single treatment after stroke rescued plastic in both paradigms, while treatment twice diminished plastic-promoting effect
MMPs play a crucial role in adult plastic and MMPactivity must be within a narrow window for experience plastic to occur

**Title:** Conformational Dynamics of AMPA Receptors at Vertebrate Excitatory Synapses: Insights from Cryo-EM and Chemical Cross-Linking Studies

**Research Question:** How do auxiliary subunits influence the conformational flexibility of AMPA receptors at vertebrate excitatory synapses?

**Methods:** We employed cryo-electron microscopy and bifunctional methaniosulfate cross-linking to investigate the conformational dynamics of AMPA receptors in the presence and absence of the auxiliary subunit Stargin.

**Findings:** Our data indicate that the extracellular layer of AMP receptors can be trapped in stable, opened conformations upon long exposures to glutamate, whereas Stargin limits this conformation flexibility. Under synaptic conditions, where glutamate and auxiliary proteins are present, the extracellular domains of AMP receptors likely remain compact.

**Implications:** Our results suggest that extracellular domains of AMP receptors are unlikely to undergo large structural rearrangements during synaptic transmission and operate in a compact conformation regime, unless exposed to long glutamate in pathological conditions.

**Conclusions:** The presence of auxiliary subunits, such as Starg, restricts conformational flexibility of AMP receptors, limiting their ability to undergo large-scale structural changes during synaptic transmission. Our findings provide new insights into the structural dynamics of AMP receptors at vertebrate excitatory synapses and highlight the role of auxiliary subunits in regulating receptor conformation.

**Title:** Unraveling the Mysteries of Oxidative Stress in Aging: A Novel Insights from Chronological Yeast Aging

**Research Question:** What is the role of oxidative stress in chronological aging, and how does it contribute to the aging process?

**Methods:** We employed chronological aging Saccharomyces cerevisiae as a model system to investigate the onset, extent, and targets of protein oxidation in post-mitotic cells. We monitored the thiol status of almost 300 proteins and discovered that cells undergo a redox collapse leading to massive oxidation of 80% of identified proteins several days before cell death.

**Findings:** Contrary to the free radical theory of aging, we found that oxidative stress is not triggered by an increase in endogenous oxidants, but by a substantial drop in NAD+ levels, which normally maintains cellular redox homeostasis. This decrease in NAD levels occurs early in lifespan and sets off a cascade downregulation of cellular processes. Caloric restriction, a universal lifespan-extending measure, increases NAD levels and delays each facet of the cascade. Our studies reveal a timeline of events leading to system-wide oxidation of the proteome before cell death.

**Implications:** Our results suggest that chronological aging represents a physiological stress condition that utilizes the redox buffering capacity of the thiolome. We cannot exclude that oxidation of some proteins might contribute to beneficial downregulation of cellular processes that shorten lifespan. The decrease in NAD levels and concomitant increase in redox potential is not yeast-specific, but shared with other organisms' metabolism. Yeast undergo same pattern NAD decrease, T1 oxidation, and red collapse (time-delayed) when cultivated in 2% or 0 glucose.

**Conclusions:** This study provides novel insights into the role of oxidative stress in chronological aging, revealing a global red collapse of thiol-containing proteins before cell death. Our findings highlight the importance of NAD levels in maintaining redox homeostasis and suggest caloric restriction as a potential therapeutic strategy to delay aging. The chronological lifespan of S. cerevisiae represents a model for aging postmit cells, allowing us to uncover protein targets of oxidative modifications contributing to aging and establish correlation between oxidative stress and lifespan.

**Title:** Unveiling the Mechanism of Spontaneous Gating in Cystic Fibrosis Transmembrane Conductance Regulator (CFTR) Channels: Energetic Coupling between Nucleotide Binding Domains (NBDs) and Pore Movements

**Research Question:** What is the molecular mechanism underlying spontaneous gating in cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) channels, a chloride channel mutated in cystic fibrosis (CF) patients?

**Methods:** We investigated the energetic coupling between nucleotide binding domains (NBDs) and pore movements in CFTR channels using single-channel recordings and molecular dynamics simulations.

**Findings:** Our results show that spontaneous openings in CFTR channels are strictly coupled to NBD dimerization, which may occur even without coordinated NBD/pore movements. This suggests that NBD movements are intrinsic to CFTR, altering the stability but not the structural architecture of open and closed conformations. This explains the effects of phosphorylation, mutations, and drugs on ATP-driven and spontaneous activity, providing insights into CF and drug mechanisms.

**Implications:** Our findings constrain mechanistic models of ATP-dependent gating and driving forces conserved among ATP-binding cassette (ABC) proteins. They also explain modulatory effects of physiological or pharmacological regulators.

**Conclusions:** The presence of bound ATP does not alter global conformations; rather, it acts as a molecular glue to stabilize the transition and open state. The mechanism of ATP binding promotes CFTR opening is likely through stabilizing the high-energy transition state, not altering the arrangement of conformations. The occlusion of the ligand site in open channels precludes open-state binding, making rates zero. Disruption of hydrolysis forces CF channels to gate, following a linear sequence rather than a cycle. Low-probability ATP-independent openings can be linked to N dimer, but our results argue that remaining N-TMD machinery undergoes similar movements to dimer when truncated.

**Key Takeaways:**

* Spontaneous openings in CFTR channels are coupled to NBD dimer, which may occur without coordinated NBD/pore movements.
* NBD movements are intrinsic to CFTR, altering stability but not structural architecture of open and closed conformations.
* ATP binding stabilizes the transition and open state, not altering conformations.
* Mechanism of ATP binding promotes opening is through stabilizing high-energy state, not altering conformations.
* Ligand site occlusion in open channels precludes open binding, making rates zero.
* Disruption hydroly forces CF to gate, following linear sequence rather than cycle.

**Title:** Unveiling the "Orderobic" Effect: A Novel Mechanism for Transmembrane Protein Assembly in Lipid Bilayers

**Research Question:** How do transmembrane proteins assemble in lipid bilayers, and what is the underlying mechanism driving force behind this process?

**Methods:** Using large-scale molecular simulations, we investigated the behavior of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers, focusing on the transition between ordered and disordered phases. We considered proteins with hydrophobic thickness equal to that of the disordered phase embedded in an ordered bilayer.

**Findings:** Our results show that a protein with hydrophobic thickness equal to that of the disordered phase embedded in an ordered bilayer stabilizes a microscopic order-disorder interface with finite stiffness. When such proteins approach each other, assembly reduces the interfacial energy, analogous to the hydroobic effect. We refer to this phenomenon as the "orderobic" effect. This effect is mediated by proximity to the order-disorder transition and hydroph mismatch of the protein. The strength and range of forces arising from this effect are significantly larger than those from membrane elasticity.

**Implications:** The orderobic effect is a novel mechanism for transmembrane protein assembly in lipid bilayers, distinct from the hydroobic effect. The strength and range of orderobic forces depend on the ability of the protein to induce disorder in its vicinity, which depends on proximity to the membrane transition and hydroph mismatch. Our prediction of enhanced lipid mobility surrounding orderobic proteins may be experimentally tested by single-molecule techniques. Biological membranes and transmem proteins are more complex than our models, with multiple components allowing multiple order-dis transitions.

**Conclusions:** Our study reveals a new mechanism for transmem protein assembly in lipid bilayers, mediated by the orderobic effect. This effect arises from proximity to the order-dis transition and hydroph mismatch of protein. The strength and range of orderobic forces are significantly larger than those from membrane elasticity. Our findings have implications for understanding transmembrane protein behavior and lipid bilayer dynamics, with potential applications in biotechnology and biomedicine.

* NT and CTD are responsible for vesicle docking and fusion, respectively, while MD regulates SN assembly and fusion.
* M-18 initiates SN assembly and stabilizes the half-zipped complex, independent of NT.

This study highlights the importance of M18 in regulating SN assembly and exyt, and provides insights into the mechanisms of SN assembly and fusion.

**Methods:** We employed fluorescence correlation spectroscopy (FCS) and fluorescence recovery after photobleaching (FRAP) to investigate the cytosolic state of integrin-adesome components and their dynamic exchange between adhesion sites and cytos.

**Findings:** Our results reveal that integrin adomes are extensively pre-assembled in the cytos as multi-protein building blocks for adhesion sites. Stationary adhesions symmetrically release the same types of protein complexes they recruit, maintaining a uniform cytosolic pool of building blocks. We propose a model where multi-protein blocks enable rapid and modular assembly of adhesion and symmetric exchange of blocks preserve their specifications, ensuring the logic of the system.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that integrin adomes are pre-assembled in the cytos, forming multi-pro building blocks that facilitate rapid and modular assembly of adhesion sites. Physical associations between adhesion components are spatially uniform around focal adhesions, indicating symmetric exchange of blocks with cytos without alteration. In the cytos, blocks cannot form larger structures due to protein interactions dependencies. We propose that the plasma membrane system can switch to self-assemble adhesion sites through checkpoints inducing additional interactions in integrin adomes.

**Conclusions:** Integrin adomes are preassembled in the cytos, forming multi-pro building for cell-matrix adhesion sites. Our results indicate blocks are size-confined and correspond to functional layers of focal adhesions anchoring, integrin regulation, force, and actin. Symmetric exchange between adhesion and cytos ensures blocks enter and exit sites unaltered. Our findings provide insights into the assembly logic of adhesion sites, highlighting the importance of interaction dependencies across integrin adomes.

**Title:** Electrophysiological Markers of Reduced Arousal States: A Novel Insights into Wakefulness, Sleep, and Anesthesia

**Research Question:** Can we identify a common electrophysiological marker that tracks states of reduced arousal, including sleep and anesthesia in humans?

**Methods:** We analyzed intracran and surface EEG recordings from four independent datasets to investigate the relationship between the 1/f spectral slope of the electrophys power and arousal states. Specifically, we hypothesized that the slope would become more negative (i.e., the spectrum steeper) in sleep and under anesthesia compared to wakefulness.

**Findings:** Our results demonstrate that the 1/f slope of electroph power, which reflects the non-oscillatory, scale component of neural activity, delineates wakefulness from propof anesthesia, NREM, and REM sleep. Critically, the slope discriminates wake from sleep solely on the basis of neurophysiological brain state. We also show that the slope mirrors rapid changes in excitability over the course of slow waves, providing a mechanistic link between the slope and synchrony during oscillations.

**Implications:** Our findings provide a novel electrophys marker that tracks arousal states of reduced, including sleep and anesthesia in humans. This marker, the 1/f slope of elect power, is observable on a wide range of timescales from sub-epochs to full recordings and correlates with all stages of arousal, including REM, NREM, and propof anesthesia. Our results support the notion that reduced states are not only defined by synchronous activity, but also by non-oscillatory components.

**Conclusions:** Together, our results from four studies provide five main advances: (1) the slope tracks arousal in propof anesthesia and sleep in humans with high precision; (2) the slope correlates with all stages of arousal; (3) the slope mirrors changes in excitability over slow waves; (4) the slope provides a mechanistic link between slope and synchrony; and (5) the slope differentiates wake from all states of reduced arousal. Our electroph marker, the 1/f slope of power, offers a new perspective on understanding reduced states, including sleep and anesthesia in humans.
