**Title:** Stromules Extend along Microtubules and Actin Filaments to Facilitate Chloroplast Movement during Innate Immunity

**Research Question:** How do stromules, dynamic tubular extensions of chloroplasts, interact with microtubules (MTs) and actin filaments (AFs) to facilitate chloroplast movement during innate immunity?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of MTs and AFs in stromule dynamics and chloroplast movement in Arabidopsis thaliana leaves using live-cell imaging and biochemical assays.

**Findings:** Our results show that MTs are required for stromule extension and movement, with depolymerization leading to strom retraction and stabilization increasing strom frequency. Actin filaments are not necessary for strom extension but provide anchor points for stromules. We observed a strong correlation between strom direction and chloroplast movement, with strom-directed chloroplast movement occurring in steady-state conditions without immune induction.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that MTs and AFs facilitate perinuclear clustering of chloroplasts during innate response. We propose a model where stromules extend along MTs and attach to AF points, guiding chloroplasts to the nucleus.

**Conclusions:** This study provides mechanistic insights into interactions between the cytoskeleton and stromules, revealing a new type of organellar movement along MTs and a mechanism for peruclear clustering during innate immunity. Future research will investigate molecular components required for strom dynamics and importance of peruclear clustering during immunity.

**Title:** Predicting Protein-Protein Interactions across Interfaces using Residue Covariance: A Pseudo-Likelihood Approach

**Research Question:** Can residue identities that covary across protein interfaces during evolution be used to predict contacts and assemble models of biological complexes?

**Methods:** We employed a pseudo-likelihood-based method to identify residue pairs that covary across protein-protein interfaces in the 50S ribosomal unit and 28 bacterial complexes with known structures. We found that these pairs are almost always in contact in the complex, provided the number of aligned sequences is greater than the average of the two proteins. We applied this method to predict subunit contacts for 36 complexes with unknown structures and presented models for the tripart ATP-independent periplasm (TR) transporter, tripart efflux system, pyruvate lyase-activating enzyme, and methine ABC transporter.

**Findings:** Our results with the 50S ribosome and benchmark suggest that interactions can be accurately predicted across proteinprotein interfaces given sufficient aligned sequences. Residue-residue predictions for 36 complexes with unknown structures are provided, with E. coli sequences clustered, representing multiple pairs. We used pseudo-likelihood to predict contacts for evolutionarily conserved complexes with unknown structures and presented models for four well-constrained complexes. In a set of known structures, cov pairs are almost always in 3D, provided sufficient sequences. Significant residue-residue occurs frequently between interacting pairs but rarely non-interacting, useful for predicting interactions.

**Implications:** This study examines residue-residue covariance across protein interfaces using a pseudo-likelihood statistical method. coli pairs. Predictions contribute to structures biologically important complexes.

**Conclusions:** Our findings demonstrate that residue covariance can be used to predict protein-protein interactions across interfaces and assemble models biological complexes. The pseudo-likelihood method accurately identifies covary pairs in 50S ribosome and 28 complexes, and predicts subunit contacts for 36 with unknown structures. Residue-residue covariance occurs frequently between interacting pairs, but rarely non-interacting, making it useful for predicting interactions. This study contributes to understanding protein-protein interactions and structure determination of biologically important complexes.

**Title:** Active Sensing in Visual Scenes: Unveiling the Role of Prior Knowledge and Perceptual Noise in Eye Movements

**Research Question:** How do participants accumulate information from multiple locations in a scene when performing a visual categorization task, and what is the role of prior knowledge and perceptual noise in their eye movements?

**Methods:** We employed a gaze-contingent paradigm in a categorization task, where participants' eye movements were tracked while they viewed a series of visual patterns. We used a Bayesian observer model to estimate the information gathered at each fixation and compared it to an optimal active algorithm that revealed locations to participants.

**Findings:** Our results show that participants' eye movements follow an active strategy that incorporates prior knowledge of scene structure and statistical patterns. Notably, categorization performance improved when locations were revealed by an optimal active algorithm. Using a combination of Bayesian observer and sensor, we estimated that 70% of apparent optimality in fixation arises from prior biases, perceptual noise, and inaccuracies in eye movements.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that participants select eye movements to maximize information about abstract categories, integrating information from multiple locations. This extends the range of active sensing tasks to non-spatial features, such as categories, and provides the first fixation-by-fixation analysis of information gathering under eye movements. We dissociate eye-movement process from perceptual and decision processes, identifying sources of suboptim in sensing and quantify efficiency of each fixation.

**Conclusions:** Our approach enables multiple tasks in perceptual domains. To study eye movements quantitatively, we estimated efficiency on a fixation-fixation basis in a high-level task. We focus on pattern categorization, different from search, where a single location has all information. Participants' movements were more efficient than heuristic strategies and 70% as optimal with prior, perception, noise, and saccadic inaccuracies. Inspired, we designed an experiment with controlled patterns and simple, ensuring fove vision dominated by gaze-conting categorization.

**Title:** The Helical Shape of Helicobacter pylori: Unveiling the Molecular Mechanisms of Curvature Generation and Maintenance

**Research Question:** What are the molecular mechanisms underlying the helical shape of Helicobacter pylori and how does it maintain sidewall curvature during growth?

**Methods:** We employed quantitative 3D microscopy and immunofluorescence to investigate PG patterning in H. pyl using metabolic probes and superresolution imaging. We analyzed the localization of cytoskeletal proteins Mre and C in H. pyl and compared with straight-rod E. coli.

**Findings:** Our results show that wall growth is enhanced at sidew curvature extremes, with M being most abundant at negative curvature and C being most at positive curvature. Strains expressing C variants with altered polymerization properties lose helical shape and associated curvatures. We propose a model where M and promote PG synthesis at positive and negative curvatures, respectively, and patterning is necessary for helical shape.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that M and C play crucial roles in PG patterning, enabling H. pyl to maintain sidew curvature during growth. We propose that M promotes synthesis at negative curvature due to its preference for this curvature in H. pyl and role in localizing synthesis in other organisms. To compensate for M-driven straight-rod growth, H pyl uses C to enhance synthesis at the major axis, independent of M. pyl also leverages C to promote positive curv on the sidew and supplements M-enhanced synthesis at negative curv.

**Conclusions:** We demonstrate that M and C help maintain PG activity and patter is a fundamental mechanism in helical shape maintenance. We also show that H pyl achieves broader sidew curv than curved-rod C. cresus and V. cholera and uses distinct mechanisms to achieve these curv. Our study provides insights into the molecular basis of H. pyl shape and highlights the importance of PG patter in bacterial morphogenesis.

We identify a role of CDC in mitochondrial fusion as part of a novel enzymatic cascade of CDC, UBP, and U2. CDC negatively regulates UBP, which negatively regulates U2, explaining the two Ds opposite effects on targets and ubiquostasis.

**Title:** Categorical Organization of the Ventral Occipital-Temporal Cortex (VOT) in Sighted and Blind Individuals: An fMRI Study

**Research Question:** Does the ventral occipitaltemporal cortex (VOT) exhibit categorical organization in response to auditory stimuli in sighted and congenitally blind individuals, and if so, what are the similarities and differences in the representational structure and connectivity between the two groups?

**Methods:** We employed functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to investigate OT responses to auditory stimuli in eight categories in sighted and congenally blind individuals. Our results showed that OT reliably encodes categories in both groups using representational structure and connectivity similar to the one found in vision. However, sound categories were more reliably encoded in the blind group using a representational format closer to the one in vision. Crucially, OT in congenally blind represents categorical membership rather than acoustic features of sounds.

**Findings:** Our results demonstrate that categorical organization similar to the one in vision can emerge in OT in the absence of visual experience, and that such response to sounds is enhanced and more stable in congenally blind. We used representational similarity analysis to show that membership is the main factor predicting representational structure of sounds in OT in congenally blind, rather than lower-level acoustic attributes of sounds.

**Implications:** Our study shows that OT encodes categorical membership of sounds from different categories in sight and congenally blind, using topography, represent format, and represent connectivity similar to the observed in response to images in vision. However, the representation of auditory stimuli in OT of congenally and sight individuals exhibits qualitative nuances compared to the organization by visual in sight people. This may be due to OT representing visual attributes in sight and acoustic in congenally due to cross-modal plasticity.

**Conclusions:** Our findings suggest that OT exhibits categorical organization in response to auditory stimuli in congenally and sight individuals, with similarities in represent structure and connectivity to the found in vision. However, the representation of auditory in congen and sight exhibits nuances compared to the organization by visual in sight. These results have implications for our understanding of cross-modal plasticity and the brain's ability to reorganize in response to sensory deprivation.

**Title:** Systematic Analysis of RNA Degradation Pathways in Saccharomyces cerevisiae Reveals Insights into RNA Processing, Regulation, and Surveillance

**Research Question:** How do RNA degradation pathways select and regulate RNA levels, and surveil aberrant or poorly functional RNAs in cells?

**Methods:** We employed photoactivatable ribonucleotide-enhanced crosslinking and immunoprecipitation (PARCLIP) to generate transcriptome-wide binding profiles of 30 RNA degradation factors involved in deadenylation, decapping, exosome-mediated degradation, and RNA surveillance pathways, including nuclear surveillance and cytoplasmic nonsense-mediated decay (NMD) in Saccharomy cerevisiae.

**Findings:** Our results reveal the distribution of degradation factors between different RNA classes, consistent with the canonical pathway for closed-loop forming mRNAs after deadenylation. Modeling mRNA half-lives suggests most factors bind intact mRNAs, whereas decapping factors are recruited for degradation, consistent with decapping being a rate-limiting step. Decapping preferentially binds mRN with non-optimal codons, consistent with degradation of inefficiently translated mRN. Global analysis suggests the surveillance machinery, including complexes N1/N3 and TR4, targets aberrant nuclear RN and snoRNAs.

**Implications:** Our findings provide a comprehensive understanding of RNA degradation in eukaryotic cells, revealing how degradation factors vary in binding specificities for classes of RN and locations on transcripts. Comparisons with published profiles of other RNA-binding factors reveal clusters that co-occupy RN or co-localize RN. Our dataset offers a rich resource for future studies of eukotic RNA pathways, mechanisms, and integration of mRNA metabolism.

**Conclusions:** Our systematic analysis of RNA degradation pathways in Sacomy cerevisiae reveals insights into RNA processing, regulation, and surveillance, and highlights the importance of decapping as a rate-limiting step in degradation. Decapping preferentially binds mRN with non-optimal codons, consistent with degradation of inefficiently translated mRN. The surveillance machinery targets aberrant RN and snoRN, underscoring its role in RNA quality control. Our findings provide a framework for understanding RNA degradation in eukotic cells, with implications for future studies of RNA metabolism and disease.

**Methods:** We used resting-state functional magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) in a large sample of toddlers with ASD and non-ASD comparison groups to investigate functional connectivity between social brain circuits, such as the default mode network (DM) and visual networks.

Increased DM-OTC hypoconnectivity was related to severity of social-communication difficulties, but only in toddlers with pronounced social-visual difficulties (GeoASD).

**Implications:** Early social-visual hypoconnectivity is a key neurobiological feature of GeoASD and may be critical for future social-communic development and new treatment targets for early intervention.

**Conclusion:** This study provides evidence for atypical functional organization of social and visual circuits in toddlers with ASD, highlighting the importance of heterogeneity in early social engagement for some, but not all, functional differences in ASD. Our findings support the idea that toddlerhood is characterized by hypoconnectivity between DM and visual circuits PVC and OTC as well as attention circuitry DAN. Eye-tracking is a simple, fast, and easy, and robust assay for identifying clinically relevant ASD subtypes, such as GeoD.

**Findings:** Aneupidy is common in wild isolates with lower-than-expected expression at many amplified genes. Gene dosage compensation functions at 10-30% of amplified genes. Genes subject to dosage are higher constraint in populations but show elevated amplification, suggesting copy variation is buffered.

**Implications:** Aneupidy provides an ecological advantage to strain YPS9 by amplifying a gene that escapes compensation.

**Conclusions:** Dosage buffers amplification through anup provides a natural, likely transient route to rapid phenotypic evolution.

In this study, we report that chromosomal amplification is common in non-laboratory yeast, inherently tolerant of anup and active dosage at transcript level for specific amplified genes. Strikingly, genes subject to dosage are buffered against copy variation (CN) and show elevated amplification in isolates. We generated isogenic aneup-euploid pairs for three wild strains to distinguish between heritable downregulation and dosage compensation. Our results provide insight into the long-standing conundrum: how cells tolerate duplication toxic genes for new phenotypes to evolve. Up to 40% amplified genes in anup strains show lower-than-expected expression due to heritable polymorphisms downregulating amplified genes, some selected during adaptation to anupidy.

We investigated transcript abundance relative to DNA for amplified genes, interrogating 220 across six anupids. Results raise the possibility of natural variation in dosage compensation. Dosage-compensated expression is controlled in proportion to copy, but many escape compensation in anup and contribute to phenotyp variation. Whole and partial amplification is common in laboratory experiments.

**Title:** Elucidating the Mechanism of P2 Receptor Gating through Photo-Switchable Cross-Linkers and Molecular Dynamics Simulations

**Research Question:** How does the P2 receptor open its transmembrane pore in response to extracellular ATP, and what is the molecular mechanism of pore opening?

**Methods:** We employed photo-switchable cross-linkers, termed "molecular tweezers," to monitor inter-residue distances in the transmembrane domain of P2 receptor during activation. Experimentally derived structural constraints were combined with computational studies to provide high-resolution models of the channel in open and closed states.

**Findings:** Our data reveal that the extent of outer pore expansion is significantly reduced compared to the ATP-bound structure. Moreover, the inner and outer ends of adjacent pore-lining helices come closer during opening, likely through a hinge-bending motion.

**Implications:** These findings provide new insight into the gating of P2 receptors and establish a versatile strategy applicable to membrane proteins.

**Conclusions:** This study presents the first application of photoable derivatives to investigate the mechanism of pore gating in P receptors. Our results demonstrate that a decrease in distance between the inner and outer ends of adjacent poreining helices, through bending at a critical gating hinge, is essential for activation of the P2 receptor. This is consistent with previous studies showing that breaking the TM-TM interface is sufficient for gating, but contradicts evidence that this movement alters sensitivity to ATP, suggesting a local rearrangement of the TMD. Our study provides a new chemical method to explore the motion of the rat2 receptor by cross-linking cysteine residues with photoable azobenz tweez, whose end-to-end distance can be controlled by light. A set of structural constraints with Molecular Dynamics (MD) provided high-resolution models of the channel in both open and closed states in physiological environment.

**Title:** Cell-Type Variation in Cytokinesis Regulation in the Four-Cell C. elegans Embryo: Intrinsic and Extrinsic Mechanisms Promote Robust Division

**Research Question:** How do individual cells within the four-cell C. elegans embryo regulate cytokinesis, the physical division of a cell into two, and what are the molecular mechanisms underlying cell-type variation in this process?

**Methods:** We used fast-acting temperature-sensitive mutants and acute drug treatment to probe the mechanisms of cell-type variation in cytokinesis among blastomeres within the four-cell C. elegans embryo. We employed thermogenetics, drug, embryo dissection, and microsection to investigate the regulation of cytokinesis in individual cell types.

**Findings:** We found cell-type differences in the functional and temporal window of activity forminK-1, but not myosin-IIN-2, during cytokinesis. In one-cell embryos, cytokinesis failed 100% of the time with formin-1 inactivation by thermal shift to restrictive temperature at any time before mid-constriction. In contrast, four-cell embryos showed cell variation in the requirement formin-1 activity among individual blastomeres. Our analysis revealed cell-type variation in the requirement formin-1, but not myos-I-2, in four-cell embryos.

**Implications:** Our data demonstrate that both cell-intrinsic and extrinsic mechanisms contribute to cytoketic diversity and promote robust cytokinesis when the contractile ring is weakened in specific cell types within the four C. elegans embryo. Each cell has a unique identity and can be individually scored contractile constriction during cytokinesis.

**Conclusions:** This study reveals cell-type variation in cytokinesis in the four C. elegans embryo, challenging the long-held assumption that all animal cells divide by a similar mechanism. Our findings highlight the importance of cell-specific regulation in cytokinesis and suggest that intrinsic and extrinsic mechanisms work together to ensure robust division when the contractile ring is weakened. These results have implications for understanding cell division in development and disease, where contractile ring failure can lead to developmental abnormalities.

**Conclusions:** The flagellar sheath plays a previously unrecognized role in the release of immunogenic LPS from V. fischer and V. cholera, promoting host-micro interactions and immune responses. Further research will elucidate the mechanisms of squid-vrio symbiosis, including LPS composition, squid receptors, and flagellin's role in host responses. This study expands our understanding of the enigmatic flagellar sheath and its contribution to bacterial virulence.

**Title:** Apoptotic Caspases: A Double-Edged Swords in Tumor Suppression and Promotion**

**Research Question:** How do apoptotic caspases, traditionally regarded as tumor suppressors, promote tumor growth and proliferation under certain conditions, and how are cells protected from their harmful effects?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of apoptotic caspases in a well-studied neoplastic tumor model, Drosoph, with oncogenic mutations in the proto-oncogene RasV (Ras12), which maintains tumorous cells in an 'und-like condition and transforms caspases from suppressors into promotors.

**Findings:** Our results show that caspases are activated in Ras12-modified tumorous cells, promoting the generation of intra- and extracellular reactive oxygen species (ROS), which recruit and activate macrophage-like immune cells that signal back to tumorous epithelial cells, activating oncogenic JNK signaling. JNK further amplifies casase activity, constituting a feedback amplification loop. Interfering with this loop reduces neoplastic behavior and improves organism survival.

**Implications:** Our study reveals that Ras12-modified casases initiate a feedback loop between tumorous epithelial cells and macrophage-like cells necessary for uncontrolled growth and invasive behavior.

**Conclusions:** Although apoptotic casases are considered tumor suppressors, under certain conditions, such as oncogenic Ras12 in scri mutant cells, they can adopt tumor-promoting roles. This work extends previous models of E and J signaling switch from anti-tumor to pro-tumor by oncogenic Ras and identifies casases as essential components.

**Title:** Temporal Perception of Social Interactions: The Role of Oxytocin and Social Competency

**Research Question:** Does the subjective experience of time during social interactions vary with social competency, and is oxytocin involved in mediating this effect?

**Methods:** We conducted a series of experiments to investigate the relationship between social competency, temporal perception, and oxytocin. In Experiment 1, we measured the duration of social interactions between communicative and non-communicative agents. Results showed that communicative interactions were perceived as shorter than non-communicative, and this effect was negatively correlated with autistic-like tendencies. Experiment 2 tested the effect of intranasal oxytocin administration on socially proficient individuals, which restored the temporal compression effect. Conversely, administration of atosiban, an oxytoc antagonist, diminished the effect in socially proficient individuals. Experiment 3 reaffirmed the influence of social interactions on subjective duration and tied it to a stable trait - social proficiency. Experiment 4 probed the role of oxytoc in social interactions by administering atos to socially proficient individuals, which diminished the effect. Experiment 5 tested if intranasal oxytoc administration would promote social-induced compression in less proficient individuals.

**Findings:** Our results indicate that perceived time is not a faithful representation of physical time, but is idiosyncratic and ingrained with personality. Oxytoc is involved in mediating time perception of social interactions, supporting its role in human cognition. Temporal compression of social interactions is negatively correlated with autistic-like traits, which can be ameliorated with intranas oxytoc administration.

**Implications:** These findings suggest that oxyin plays a critical role in social cognition, particularly in the perception of time during social interactions. Socially proficient individuals are more susceptible to temporal compression, whereas less proficient are less so. Intranas oxy administration promotes compression in less individuals, whereas atos diminishes in proficient. This highlights the complex relationship between oxyin, social competency, and time perception.

**Conclusion:** Our study demonstrates that subjective experience of time during social interactions varies with social competency, and oxyin is involved in mediating this effect. Oxy plays a critical role in social cognition, particularly in the perception of time during interactions. These findings have implications for understanding social cognition, autism, and oxyin's role in mediating social behavior.

**Research Question:** Despite the widespread occurrence of hetery in flowering plants, the genetic and evolutionary origins of this trait remain poorly understood. In Primula, hetery is controlled by the Slocus supergene, which determines reciprocal reproductive organ incompatibility between morphs. However, the identities of the component genes remain unknown.

**Methods:** We investigated the genetic basis of hetery in Primula by identifying the CYP50 gene, a putative brassinoster-degrading enzyme, as the locus determining style length polymorphism. CYP50 is present on the short-styled morph haplotype, specifically expressed in short styles, and its loss leads to long styles.

**Findings:** Our results indicate that CYP50 represents the locus in the Prim hetery super, whose expression in short styles causes brassoster degradation, limiting cell expansion and elongation. Evolutionarily, C50 arose via lineage-specific duplication and gain of style-specific expression. C50 is a rare example of an identified locus underlying adaptive polymorphism, opening the way for understanding the origin of the Prim Sloc as a complex super.

**Implications:** Our findings provide a mechanistic explanation for Prim style dimorphism and shed light on the evolution of the Sloc as a model for a plant super. The C50 gene shows accelerated molecular evolution and is absent in long-styled morphs, supporting its role in determining style length. The presence/absence polymorphism strictly cosegregates with morph in heterous species across the Prim phylogeny, strongly supporting C50 as the locus of the heter super.

**Conclusions:** Our study identifies C50 as the causal locus of heter in Primula, providing insights into the evolution of the Sloc as a complex super. This discovery opens avenues for understanding the genetic basis of heter in flowering plants and sheds light on the evolution of complex traits in plants.

**Title:** Bone Morphogenetic Protein Signaling Regulates Pluripotency and Mesodermal Differentiation through Downregulation of CDH1

**Research Question:** How does bone morphogenetic protein (BMP) signaling contribute to the maintenance of pluripotency and favor mesodermal differentiation, and what are the underlying mechanisms that control differentiation?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of BMP signaling in pluripotency and mesodermal differentiation by examining the expression of CDH1, a transcription factor involved in early stages of neural and mesodermal differentiation. **Implications:** These findings suggest that differentiation depends not only on exposure to extrinsic cues but also on morphogenetic events controlling receptivity to those cues. **Conclusions:** Our study identifies loss of CD1 as a limiting event for earliest stages of neural differentiation and proposes that BMP controls differentiation by maintaining CD1 expression. **Summary:** We have identified loss of CD1 activity as limiting for earliest stages of differentiation and propose BMP controls by maintaining CD1 expression. Our findings help explain why BMP can protect plurotency and promote mesermal differentiation at the same time.

**Title:** Centriole-associated protein Cep120 regulates centrosome homeostasis and ciliary assembly during quiescence

**Research Question:** How are the asymmetric properties of centrioles established and maintained during quiescence, and what are the underlying molecular mechanisms?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of centri-associated protein C120 (Cep) in regulating centros homeostasis and ciliary assembly during quies in mouse and human cells.

**Findings:** Depletion of C120 in quies cells leads to accumulation of pericentriolar material (PCM) components, including pericentr, C5ap2, ninein, and C170, resulting in increased microtubule-nucleation at the centome. Loss of C120 causes aberrant dyin-dependent trafficking of centomal proteins, dispersal of centolar satellites, and defective ciliary assembly and signaling.

**Implications:** Our results indicate that C120 maintains centros homeasis by inhibiting untimely maturation of the daughter centole and defines a new molecular mechanism underlying the pathogenesis of ciliopathies such as Jeune Thoracic Dystrophy and Joub syndrome.

**Conclusions:** C120 plays a critical role in regulating PCM levels during quies, and its loss initiates a series of changes in protein localization and abundance that result in defective ciliary assembly and function. Changes in the maturation state of the centole, on which C120 is enriched, disrupt ciliary assembly and signaling, a process requiring CS-dependent delivery of factors to the centome. We propose that C regulates asymmetric distribution of PCM at the centome by residing on the outer wall of centolar microtubules. Loss of C during quence is detrimental to ciliary function, likely due to aberrant CS trafficking of proteins to the centome. Collectively, our results suggest loss of C disrupts localization of CS due to changes in PCM, microtule organization, and dyin trafficking of centomal proteins. Since satellites are essential in cogenesis, we hypothesize dispersal of CS upon C loss disrupts formation and/or of cilia during quence. Our findings establish exquisite control of centome homeasis and its relationship to ciliary assembly and signaling, and uncover a new molecular defect potentially underlying pathogenesis ofili such as Je and Joub syndrome.

**Title:** Neuron-Glia Interactions in the Developing Retina: Unveiling the Molecular Pathways of Communication between Neurons and Müller Glial Cells

**Research Question:** How do neurons and Müller glial cells (MCs) interact in the developing retina, and what are the molecular pathways that mediate this communication?

**Methods:** We employed whole-cell recordings of retinal ganglion cells (RGCs) and two-photon imaging of calcium indicators and glutamate sensors specifically expressed in MCs to investigate neurotransmitter release during retinal waves and their effects on MC activity.

**Findings:** Our results show that retinal waves induce calcium transients in a large proportion of MCs, indicating that spontaneous activity encompasses both neuronal and glial networks at this age. During cholinergic retinal waves, MC transients were blocked by muscarinic acetylcholine antagonists, whereas glutamaterg waves were inhibited by ionotropic glutamate antagonists, suggesting that MC responsiveness changes match the neurotransmitter used in retinal waves. Using an optical glutamate sensor, we found that the decline in MC transients was caused by a reduction in glutamate reaching MCs.

**Implications:** These findings indicate that neurons and MC exhibit correlated activity during a critical period of retinal maturation enabled by neurotransmitter overflow from retinal synapses. Our study elucidates the spatio-temporal characteristics and molecular pathways of neuron-MC communication in the developing retina.

**Conclusions:** Our results demonstrate that MC express multiple neurotransmitter receptors throughout this period, allowing neuronal control of MC activity to be sustained as neuronal shifts from cholinergic to glutamgic signaling. The proportion of MC that responded to waves with transients decreased from P9 to P11, despite retinal waves presence. MC express a variety of neurotransmitter types, including glutamg and cholin receptors, and calcium trans at different ages could be evoked by neurotrans released during retinal. Our study provides new insights into the mechanisms of neuron-MC interactions in the developing retina and highlights the importance of neurotransmitter spillo from retinal synapses in retinal maturation.

**Title:** Establishing a Novel Microbial-Based Method for Estimating Postmortem Interval (PM) in Forensic Investigations

**Research Question:** Can microbial communities associated with decomposing corpses provide a reliable and accurate method for estimating postmortem interval (PM) in forensic investigations, overcoming the limitations of existing techniques such as forensic entomology?

**Methods:** We conducted a laboratory experiment to investigate the temporal changes in microbial communities associated with mouse corpses decomposing on soil over 48 days. We characterized bacteria, archaea, and eukaryotes using a combination of culture-independent, high-throughput DNA sequencing approaches for each sample.

**Findings:** Our results demonstrate that postmortem microbial changes are dramatic, measurable, and repeatable in a mouse model, allowing PM to be estimated within approximately 3-48 days. We provide a detailed understanding of bacterial and eukaryotic ecology within a decomposing system, suggesting that microbial data can be developed into a tool for estimating PM.

**Implications:** Our study rigorously tests hypotheses held by the forensic community about the role of microbes in decomposition and assesses whether microbial changes are predictable over the timeline, crucial for using microbes as a "clock" to estimate PM. Our data not only understand the underlying ecology of decomposition but also the feasibility of using microbes as evidence. We demonstrate that microbial community change is measurable and directional during decomposition, allowing accurate estimates of past events such as PM.

**Conclusions:** This proof-of-principle study shows that microbial ecology of decomposing corpses has potential to be developed into a complementary forensic tool for estimating PM. Our approach distinguishes from prior work by evaluating models' ability to explain variation in data, being fit to all points (Pech et al., 2013). We provide a novel method for estimating PM that avoids limitations of existing techniques, such as forensic entom, which can range from days to months. Microbes offer a promising alternative for estimating PM in forensic investigations.

Using QDs, we found no accumulation of large extrasynaptic receptors, instead, motion is constrained in PSD nanodom.

**Title:** Mechanism of Hypoxia-Induced Increase in Voltage-Gated Sodium Current (IN) in Central Neurons: Role of SUMOylation of NaV1.2 Channels**

**Research Question:** What is the molecular mechanism underlying the increase in voltage-gated sodium current (IN) in response to hypoxia in central neurons?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of the Small Ubiquitin-like Modifier (SUM) pathway in rat cerebellar granule neurons (CG) and its effect on Na1.2 channels in response to hypia.

**Findings:** Our results show that hypia activates the SUM pathway in CG, leading to SUMylation of Na1.2 channels, which increases IN. The time-course of SUMylation of Na1.2 channels at the surface and IN coincide, and are prevented by mutation of Na1-Lys38 or application of a deSUMylating enzyme. Within 40s, hypia linkage of SUM1 to channels is complete, shifting the voltage dependence of channel so that depolarizing steps evoke larger sodium currents.

**Implications:** Given the recognized role of IN in hypoxic brain damage, the SUM pathway and Na1.2 are identified as potential targets for neuroprotective interventions.

**Conclusions:** Our findings suggest that SUMylation of Na.2 channels at the plasma membrane underlies the immediate increase IN response to hypia in cultured neurons and CHO cells. This is consistent with tonic control of IN by the SUM pathway, as SUM and SEN1 increased and decreased the current, respectively, under normoxic conditions. The response IN to hypia was ablated by µ-Conotoxin-TII (CTX), a blocker of Na. channels. Supporting the mechanism, hypia SUMylation of Na.2 was shown to increase the interaction of SUM and Na. Our results suggest that Na. channels and SUM enzymes may be valuable targets for reducing damage due to acute hypoxic insults to central neurons.

**Title:** Elucidating the Thermal Optima and Limits of Vector-Borne Disease Transmission: A Mechanistic Modeling of Ross River Virus Transmission

**Research Question:** What are the thermal optima and limits of vector-borne disease transmission, and how do they vary among different vector-pathogen systems?

**Methods:** We developed a mechanistic model for the thermal response of Ross River virus (RV), an important mosquito-borne pathogen in Australia and Pacific Islands, using data from two vector species (Culex annulirostris and Ae. vigil). We parameterized the model with empirical, unimodal thermal responses to predict transmission.

**Findings:** Our results show that RV transmission peaks at moderate temperatures (26.4°C) and declines to zero at thermal limits (17.0 and 31.5°C). The model accurately predicts year-round transmission in the tropics but seasonal in temperate areas, resulting in a seasonal peak in human cases. Climate warming will increase transmission in temperate areas (where most Australians live) but decrease in tropical areas where temperatures are near the optimum.

**Implications:** These findings highlight the importance of nonlinear models in inferring the role of temperature in disease dynamics and predicting responses to climate change. Thermal responses vary substantially among diseases and vectors, yet mechanistic models are lacking for many. Our results support a general pattern of intermediate thermal optima for transmission, where optimal temperature is driven by adult lifespan, but upper and lower limits are uncertain, determined by unique vector-pathogen traits. The strongest signal of temperature on disease is in temperate regions where temperature varies along the rising portion of the R curve (~20-25°C). Upper limits of mosquito-borne transmission, a concern for climate change, are determined by vector life traits with symmetrical performance (fecundity and survival) rather than asymmetrical traits (biting or pathogen rate).

**Conclusions:** This study demonstrates the importance of mechanistic modeling in understanding thermal responses of vector-b disease. Our results illustrate the need to move beyond linear assumptions of increasing transmission with temperature, to accurately predict geographic, seasonal, and interannual variation in disease. Climate change will impact transmission differently in temperate and tropical areas, underscoring the need for region-specific predictions.

**Title:** Deciphering the Behavioral Correlates of Cortical Beta Oscillations: A Multi-Scale Neural Activity Analysis of Motor Cortical Oscillations and Behavior

**Research Question:** How are cortical beta oscillations linked to changes in neural activity and behavior?

**Methods:** We recorded multi-scale neural activity from the motor cortex of three macaques performing a novel neurofeedback task, where subjects voluntarily brought their beta power to an instructed state and executed arm reaches. Reaches preceded by a reduction in beta exhibited faster onset times than those preceded by an increase in power. Population activity was found to shift from a movement state during beta oscillations that were neurofeedback-induced or naturally occurring during reaching.

**Findings:** Our results establish a neural basis for slowed onset periods following beta oscillatory activity. Notably, this shift in population was also seen in naturally occurring oscillations during tasks, suggesting beta reflect common spiking patterns in different contexts. This study ties together behavioral correlates of oscillations with hypotheses of the motor cortex encodes movement through population-level activity.

**Implications:** Our findings support the hypothesis that beta oscillations are linked to patterns slow onset new movements (Gilbertson et al., 2005) and add to previous reports of elevated power prior and after-trained movements (Donoghue et al., 199; Mur and Fetz, 199; Sanes andogh, 199). The behavioral effect of neuro-induced oscillations matches hypotheses a movement-slow role of oscillations during natural movements (Gilbert et al., 200). However, individual unit responses during beta were different across tasks. Mechanistic generators of oscillations reconciled discrepancies in reported correlates activity.

**Conclusions:** Simultaneously recorded units exhibited different activity during neurofeedback beta compared to natural oscillations during reaching. Despite unit-level differences, population analyses show beta promote a movement-slow state both tasks, matching behaviorally observed. This study provides a population basis for understanding the neural correlates of beta oscillations and their role in motor control.

**Methods:** We investigated the function of TR2, a component of the TNF superfamily signaling complex, in regulating immune homeostasis and keratinocyte survival using TR2 deficient mice and keratinocyte-specific deletion models. This study highlights the importance of TR2 in regulating keratin survival and inflammation, and its potential as a therapeutic target for psoriasis treatment.

**Title:** Unveiling a Novel Pathway for Synaptic Vesicle Reformation Independent of Clathrin and Dynamin 1/3 during Intense Synaptic Activity

**Research Question:** How do synaptic vesicles (SVs) rapidly reform after exocytosis, and what is the role of clrin-mediated endocytosis (CME) and dynamin 1/3 in this process?

**Methods:** We used synapses of dynamin 1 and 3 double-knockout (Dyn1/3KO) neurons, where CME is impaired, to investigate the poorly understood mechanisms of SV reformation. We analyzed the formation of bulkosomes and their conversion into SVs in the presence and absence of clrin and dyn 1/3.

**Findings:** Massive formation of bulkosomes was not defective, but rather enhanced in the absence of dyn 1 and 3. The conversion of bulkosomes into SVs was not accompanied by accumulation of clrin-coated buds on their surface, and proceeded even after clrin knockdown, suggesting independence of clrin. These findings support the existence of a pathway for SVformation that bypasses the requirement for clrin and dyn 1/3 and operates during intense synaptic activity.

**Implications:** Our results reveal a novel pathway for SV reformation that is independent of clrin and dyn 1/3, which is activated during intense synaptic activity. This challenges the current understanding that clrin and dyn 1/3 are essential for SV reformation, and highlights the complexity of synaptic vesicle recycling.

**Conclusions:** Our study demonstrates that clrin and dyn 1/3 are not required for bulkocyt, and their absence delays, but does not abolish, SVformation from internal membranes. We propose a novel pathway for SVformation that bypasses clrin and dyn 1/3, which is activated during intense synaptic activity. These findings have significant implications for our understanding synaptic plasticity and the mechanisms of neurotransmitter release.

Quorum activates T1-mediated toxicity and immunity, allowing competent cells to police against mutants. In the absence of cell contact, mutants have a fitness advantage due to the cost associated with BR-dependent activities. Intact T1 apparatus is required for wild-type to outcomp mutants in agar growth. This work highlights a novel mechanism for limiting quorum mutant growth in groups and expands our understanding of T functions in Burkia thailand.

**Title:** Regulation of Skeletal Muscle Satellite Cell Quiescence by Tristrapin and p38/β MAPK Signaling

**Methods:** To investigate the mechanisms involved in maintaining satellite cell quies, we identified transcripts differentially expressed during satellite cell activation following muscle injury. **Conclusions:** Muscle regeneration requires SCs to rapidly exit quies but also renew quies SC upon completion. We propose T suppresses transcripts for activation as a mechanism for immediate exit quies. Upon injury, pαβ MAP is rapidly activated phosphorylating T, suggesting T plays a role in maintaining quies. T is present in quies SC, inhibited by pβ MAP signaling within minutes of activation, and reduces half-life MyD mRNA. Loss of T from muscle preciously activates SC, promoting My expression and aberrant cycle entry.

* Tristrol is a key regulator of satellite cell quies
* pβ MAP signaling inactivates Trist, stabilizing MyD mRNA
* Satellite cell knockdown Trist preciously activates cells, enabling My accumulation and myofib differentiation
* T suppresses MyD and other transcripts byob during quies formation or reacquisition
* pβ MAP phosphorylates T upon activation, suggesting role in quies maintenance
* T is present in quies, inhibited byβ signaling, and reduces MyD half-life

**Title:** Macrophage-Intrinsic Interleukin-10 Signaling Regulates Intestinal Homeostasis in Infant Mice and Prevents Early-Onset Colitis

**Methods:** We conducted a time-series study using IL-10 deficient mice and their littermate controls, evaluating histology, inflammatory gene expression, cellular composition of the lamina propria, and luminal bacterial communities from birth to 14 weeks of age. We used clodron depletion to investigate the role of colonic macrophages in colonic inflammation that develops in IL-10 deficient mice.

**Implications:** Our results indicate that IL-10 mediated regulation of macrophage function during postnatal period is indispensable for preventing murine colitis, suggesting that macroph-intrinsic IL-10 signaling is critical for intestinal homeostasis in infancy. The absence of IL-10 on macrophages renders mice susceptible to colitis, highlighting the importance of IL-10 mediated macrophage function in preventing intestinal inflammation.

**Conclusions:** This study demonstrates that macroph-intrinsic IL-10 signaling is required during a critical developmental transition, underscoring the importance of understanding factors that initiate colitis in infants with genetic IL-10 deficiencies. Our findings have significant implications for understanding the etiopathogenesis of infantile colitis and highlight the importance of IL-10/ILR pathway in mucosal homeostasis and prevention of intestinal inflammation in infancy.

**Title:** In Vivo Bistability of Purkin Neurons in Larval Zebraf (Danio rerio): A Novel Insights into Cerebellar Function

**Research Question:** Do Purkin neurons exhibit bistability in vivo in unanesthetized larval zraf (Danio rerio)?

**Methods:** We recorded intracellularly from Purkin neurons in unanhetized larval zraf (Dan rerio) from 4 to 19 days post-fertilization (dpf) to investigate bistability in vivo.

**Findings:** We unequivocally demonstrate bistability in Purkin neurons in vivo. Tonic firing was observed in depolarized regimes and bursting at hyperpolar membrane potentials. Purkin neurons could switch from one state to another spontaneously or with current injection. GABA or NMDA receptors were not required for bursting, but activation of AMP receptors by climbing fibers (CFs) was sufficient to trigger bursts. Recording Purkin membrane potential intracellular and motor spikes extracellular showed initiation of motor spiking correlated with increased CFs and depolarization. Bistability was observed soon after Purkin specification and persisted until late larval stages.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest bistability is a conserved feature of Purkin neurons from fish to mammals, as supported by conservation of structural and functional properties of the cerebellum. In vivo, Purkin neurons can be in tonic or bursting mode and toggle between states. This is the first demonstration of bistability in Purkin neurons in vivo in a vertebrate.

**Conclusions:** This study provides novel insights into cerebellar function and bistability in vivo. Bistability is a fundamental property of Purkin neurons in larval zraf, and likely conserved across vertebrates. Our findings have implications for understanding cerebellar function and motor control in vertebrates. Future studies should investigate bistability in mature cerebellum and its role in motor learning and plasticity.

**Title:** Force Generation by Actin Assembly Shapes Cellular Membranes: An Experimentally Constrained Multiscale Model Shows a Minimal Branched Actin Network is Sufficient for Internalization of Endocytic Pits Against Membrane Tension

**Research Question:** How does actin assembly contribute to the internalization of endocytic pits against membrane tension in mammalian cells?

**Methods:** We developed a multiscale model combining continuum mechanics and filament-based simulations of actin polymerization to investigate the role of actin in endocytosis. We used cryo-electron tomography to confirm the presence of bent actin filaments in endocytic pits and measured the number of Arp/3 complexes at clath-mediated endocyt sites.

**Findings:** Our results show that a minimal branched actin network is sufficient to internalize endytic pits against physiological membrane tension. Actin self-organization and bending, arising from act-coat attachments around the endytic pit, enable the network to adapt to changing loads. Approximately 200 Arp/3 complexes accumulate at clath endocyt sites over time. Incorporating this number into our multiscale model, simulations revealed that actin self-organizes into a radial array with ends toward the base of the pit, with filaments bending between the coat and base. Elastic energy stored in bent filaments contributes to endytic internalization. Elevated tension directs filaments toward the base, increasing actin nucleation and bending force production.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that spatially constrained actin assembly utilizes an adaptive mechanism for endocyt under varying physical constraints. In cells with low tension, endyt occurs with low Arp/3 activity, consistent with previous observations (Bant et al., 2011). We propose that actin self-organization from HipR distribution and filament bending allows the endytic network to change organization and force capacity under elevated loads.

**Conclusions:** Our multiscale model demonstrates that a minimal actin network composed of act, Arp/3, and capping, with attachments in clath and constrained by biochemical measurements, generates sufficient force for endytic internalization against mammalian membrane tension. Actin self-organization and bending enable the network to adapt to changing loads, facilitating endytic under physiological conditions.

**Title:** Structural and Functional Insights into the Role of Scc in Cohesin Loading and Localization during Sister Chromosome Segregation

**Research Question:** How does the Scc protein complex contribute to the loading of cohesin onto DNA and its localization across the genome, and what is the molecular basis for its recruitment to centromeres?

**Methods:** We employed X-ray crystallography to determine the structure of Scc bound to a fragment of S2, and used yeast genetics to investigate the role of Scc in cohes loading and localization.

**Findings:** The structure reveals a TPR array on S4 that envelops an extended S2 peptide, and a conserved patch on the surface of S4 is required to recruit S2/4 to centromeres and pericentere. Perturbation of this patch eliminates centrom localization of S2 in mitotic cells and reduces association of cohes subunit S1 with centrom and pericere, but not chromosome arms.

**Implications:** Our findings establish the role of S4 in determining cohes localization and provide a molecular basis for S/4 recruitment to centrom. The conserved patch on S4 is essential for targeting cohes to a specific locus, separable from its role in establishing sister chromatid cohesion across the genome.

**Conclusions:** Scc plays a crucial role in loading cohes onto DNA and determining its localization across the genome. Defects in cohes loading lead to chromosome missegregation and aberrant transcriptional regulation, causing developmental defects in multicellular organisms. The S4-dependent cohes targeting pathway is conserved across eukaryotes, suggesting a general mechanism for cohes loading.

**Title:** Season of Birth Affects Growth, Fattening, and Winter Survival in Garden Dormice (Eliomys quinus): A Comparative Study of Early- and Late-Born Juveniles

**Research Question:** Does the timing of birth in the reproductive season influence growth, fattening, energetic responses, winter survival, and fecundity in early-born (EB) and late-born (LB) juvenile garden dormice (Eliysinus quinus)?

**Methods:** We investigated the growth, fattening, energetic responses, winter survival, and fecundity of EB and LB juvenile dormice in a controlled laboratory setting. We manipulated food availability (ad libitum vs. intermittently fasted) to examine its effects on growth and pre-hibern fattening, use of energy-saving strategies (daily torpor), body size and fat prior to hibernation, overwintering patterns, and mass, and fecundity in the following spring.

**Findings:** LB juveniles grew and gained mass twice as fast as EB individuals, with low tor use during growth, but increased during pre-hibern fattening. LB showed higher tor use, reached similar body sizes but lower fat content than EB before hibern. LB showed similar hibernation patterns, but higher proportion of breeders the following year than EB.

**Implications:** Our results suggest tor is incompatible with growth but promotes preibern fattening and consolidates preibern fat deposits in dormice. Being born late in the season is associated with a fast life history.

**Conclusions:** Our findings support the hypothesis that LB dormice grow and fatten at higher rates due to (i) higher tor use, (ii) reach preibern levels of body but lower fat, leading (iii) differences hibern patterns and mass overwinter, compared EB individuals. The use of tor seemed to be incompatible with growth but supports preibern fattening in the late season and preservation of fat previously deposited prior hibern. This was especially the case for EB that reached higher fat reserves prior hibern, compared LB. LB delayed onset hibern by 5 weeks.

**Title:** Mechanisms of Influenza A Virus-Induced Host Shutoff: Preferential Translation of Viral mRNAs and Dominance over Cellular mRNA Pool

**Research Question:** How does Influenza A virus (IAV) induce host shutoff, a strategy used by viruses to repress cellular mRNA translation and concomantly allow efficient translation of viral mRNAs?

**Methods:** We employed RNA sequencing and ribosome profiling to investigate the mechanisms utilized by IAV to induce shutoff. Our analysis revealed that viral mRNAs are preferentially translated, leading to a decline in cellular protein synthesis mediated by viral takeover of the mRNA pool.

**Findings:** Our measurements uncovered variability in levels of cellular transcript reduction, indicating that short transcripts are less affected by IAV. Interestingly, mRNAs refractory to IAV infection are enriched in maintenance processes such as oxidative phosphorylation. Continuous phosphorylation activity is crucial for viral propagation.

**Implications:** Our results advance understanding of IAV-induced shutoff and suggest a mechanism facilitating translation of genes with housekeeping functions.

**Conclusions:** Our systematic and unbiased analysis demonstrates that viralRNAs are preferentially translated compared to host counterparts, and extensive translation of viral proteins result from viral takeover of mRNA in the cell. This is consistent with notion that NS is not required for shutoff host protein production. To access host machinery, viruses utilize sh mechanisms eliminating competition from cellular transcripts. Sh could be achieved by two mechanisms: direct-opting translation by forcing viralRN better than host counterparts, or takeover of mRNA pool. IAV employs the latter strategy, with viralRN taking over 53.8% of translation activity at 8 hours post-infection, while 57.3% mRNAs in cells are viral. Reduction in cellular expression is main cause shutoff during infection. Our findings concur that PA endonuclease plays dominant role in sh during IAV infection.

**Key Takeaways:**

* IAV induces sh by preferential translation of viralRN and takeover of mRNA pool
* Short transcripts less affected by IAV
* mRN refractory to IAV enriched in maintenance processes like oxidative phosphorylation
* Continuous phosphorylation crucial for viral propagation
* PA enduclease plays dominant role in sh during IAV

**Title:** Mechanisms of Gradient Formation of Signaling Proteins in Tissue Morphogenesis: A Novel Insights from Drosophila FGF Signaling

**Research Question:** How do signaling gradients of proteins induce tissue morphogenesis, and what are the underlying mechanisms of gradient formation?

**Methods:** We investigated the distribution of fluorescently-tagged signaling proteins, FGF and FGFR, expressed at physiological levels from genomic knock-in alleles in Drosoph, focusing on the larval wing imaginal disc. We found that FGF produced in the wing disc moves to the air sac-primord (ASP) through FGFR-containing cilia that extend from ASP to contact the wing disc. The number of F-receiving cilia by ASP cells decreases with increasing distance from the source, generating a specific F gradient.

**Findings:** Acting as a morphogen in ASP, F activates concentration-dependent gene expression of Pointed1 at higher and Cut at lower levels. Transcription factors Pointed1 and Cut antagonize each other, differentially regulating FG-containing cilia formation, creating regions with higher-to-lower F-receiving cilia. These results reveal a mechanism where morphogens self-generate precise tissue-specific contours through feedback regulation of cilia-mediated dispersion.

**Implications:** Our study reveals a novel mechanism where morphogens self-generate precise tissue-specific contours through feedback regulation of cilia-mediated dispersion. This contrasts with the traditional view that paracrine signals, such as FGF proteins, function as diffusible signals forming a gradient by passive extracellular dispersion. Our findings suggest that local cilia-mediated cell-cell communication can create long-range signals to regulate tissue morphogenesis.

**Conclusions:** This study addresses fundamental questions on gradient formation of Doph FGF protein, Branchless (B), which guides branching morphogenesis of tracheal epithelial tubes. We show that B diffuses from its source to form an extracellular gradient that guides directional migration of trache branches. However, our results indicate that B gradient shape adapts to diverse morphologies through feedback regulation of cilia. Our findings demonstrate that cilia-dependent signaling can provide precision and adaptability in shaping long-range gradients and tissue architectures in space and time.

**Title:** Discovery of a Novel α/β Coiled Protein Structure through Insertion of Two or Six Residues into the Heptad Repeat of a Trimeric Coiled Coil

**Research Question:** Can the insertion of two or six residues into the hept repeat of a trimer coiled coil lead to the formation of a novel protein structure, and what are the implications of this discovery on our understanding of protein folding and design?

**Methods:** We investigated the effects of inserting two or six residues into the hept repeat of a trimer coiled coil using parametric equations to describe the backbone structure. Our results show that this insertion strains the supercoil, causing the formation of short β-strands that move 120° around the trimer axis, resulting in an α/β coiled structure retaining only one backbone bond per unit from the parent coiled.

**Findings:** Our study reveals the unexpected formation of a novel α/β coiled structure through the insertion of two or six residues into the hept repeat of trimer coiled. This structure retains only one backbone bond per unit from the parent coiled and forms a β-layer, a new supersecondary structure composed of short-strands. The α/β coiled is built of hexads with three residues in β and three in α regions of the Ramachran plot.

**Implications:** The discovery of α/β coiled structure expands our understanding of protein folding and design. α-helical coiled coils are ubiquitous, and their structure can be computed with parametric equations, making them ideal for design. However, insertions of 2 and 6 residues were thought to cause greatest strain on α-hical geometry. Our results show that these insertions break α-hices to form β-strands, which associate into β-layer, a novel supersecondary structure. The α/β coiled is a new class of fiber with alternating α and β-structure, a fold not previously described.

**Conclusions:** Our results demonstrate that minor mutations to known folds can yield novel structures within allowed regions of Ramach space. The α/ coiled is a minimalistic, retaining only single backbone bond from parent structure, built of hexads with three residues in β and three in α regions. β-layers are repetitive in natural coiled, forming regular fibers with alternating α and β-structure. The α/β coiled is a novel protein structure with potential applications in protein design and understanding.

**Title:** Investigating the Risk Factors for Asthma among School-Aged Children in Urban Uganda: A Case-Control Study

**Research Question:** What are the risk factors associated with asthma among school-aged children (5-17 years) in urban Uganda?

**Methods:** A case-control study was conducted among 555 cases and 111 controls. Asthma was diagnosed by clinicians.

**Findings:** The main risk factors for asthma were:

* Tertiary education for fathers (adjusted OR (95% CI): 2.32 (1.71-3.16) and mothers (1.85 (1.38-2.48)
* Area of residence at birth, with children born in a town or city having increased risk compared to those born in rural areas (2.16 (1.60-2.92) and 2.79 (1.79-4.35) respectively
* Father and mother's history of asthma
* Children's own allergic conditions
* Atopy
* Cooking with gas/electric

**Implications:** Asthma is associated with a rural-urban risk gradient, higher socio-economic status, and urbanicity. This study provides a basis for future studies to identify environmental/lifestyle factors responsible for increasing risk among children in urban in LMIC.

**Conclusion:** The risk of asthma among school children in urban Uganda is predicted by area of residence in life, particularly at birth, with highest risk among those whose life is spent in small towns and city. This increases in presence of other risk factors such as parental history of disease, children atopy, higher socio-economic status, and urbanicity.

**Title:** Evolution of the Olfactory System in Insects: Unveiling the Origins of Olfactory Receptors and Coreceptors

**Research Question:** How did the olfactory system evolve in insects, and what are the roles of olfactory receptors (OR) and coreceptors (Or) in this process?

**Methods:** We investigated the olfactory systems of three insect species: the wingless bristail Lepismilis signata (Archaeognatha), the firebrat Thermobia domestica (Zygentoma), and the neopter leaf insect Phyllium sifium (phasoda). We analyzed the expression of OR and Or in these species using molecular biology techniques.

**Findings:** Our results show that Lepismilis lacks OR and Or, while Thermobia has three Or candidates, and Phium has a fully developed OR/Or-based system. We suggest that OR did not arise as an adaptation to terrestrial lifestyle, but evolved later in insect evolution, with Or present before OR.

**Implications:** Our study provides new insights into the evolution of the olfactory system in insects and the role of OR and Or in this process. We propose that Or evolved after the bristail split from its common ancestor with remaining insects, and the olfactory system relying on both OR and Or emerged after the emergence of wings.

**Conclusions:** Our findings have significant implications for understanding the evolution of the olfactory system in insects and other animals. Insects provide an excellent model to study ancestral characteristics and building blocks of olfaction. Our results suggest that OR and Or are key components of the olfactory system, and that Or evolved after the brail split from common ancestor with remaining insects.

**Title:** Spatial and Temporal Analysis of Asymptomatic Malaria Infections in Villages Undergoing Mass Drug Administration in Myanmar: Evidence for a Herd Effect

**Research Question:** Can mass drug administration (MDA) of antimalarial drugs reduce the burden of asymptomatic infections and clinical episodes of Plasmodium falciparum malaria in villages, and does non-adherence to MDA hinder elimination efforts?

**Methods:** We conducted a detailed spatial and temporal analysis of malaria episodes and asymptomatic infections in four villages undergoing MDA in Myanmar, using microepidemiological and spatial data from a M trial in Kayin State, Myanmar. We investigated associations between individual and group-level participation in MDA, subclinical infections, and clinical episodes of P. falciparum post-M.

**Findings:** Individuals from neighborhoods with low MDA adherence had 2.85 times the odds of having a malaria episode post-M compared to those from high-adherence neighborhoods, regardless of individual participation, suggesting a herd effect. High mosquito rates, living with someone with malaria, or asymptomatic infection were predictors of clinical episodes. Spatial clustering of non-adherence to M, even in villages with high participation, may frustrate elimination efforts.

**Implications:** Our study provides empirical evidence for a herd effect of MDA in reducing asymptomatic and clinical P. falcum malaria, supporting the notion of a herd providing additional population protection. Non-adherence to MDA, even in villages with high participation, may hinder elimination efforts. This study contributes to the evidence base for further research, intervention, and policy.

**Conclusions:** This study demonstrates the potential of MDA in reducing asymptomatic and clinical P. falc malaria, and highlights the importance of adherence to M for herd effect. Our findings suggest that non-adherence to M, even in villages with high participation, may frustrate elimination efforts. Further research is needed to understand the impact of non-adherence on M and develop strategies to improve adherence.

**Title:** Conservation of Transcription Factor Binding Specificity in Drosophila and Mammals: Implications for the Emergence of Novel Cell Types

**Research Question:** How do transcription factor (TF) binding specificities contribute to the emergence of novel cell types in Drosila and mammals?

**Methods:** To address this question, we determined the binding specificities of 242 Dophila TFs and compared them to human and mouse data. We used HT-LEX to characterize TF binding specificities in Dophila melanogaster.

**Findings:** Our analysis revealed that TF specificities are highly conserved between Dila and mammals, and for ortholog TFs, the similarity extends to the level of subtle dinucleotide binding preferences. We found few TFs with divergent specificities in cell types not found in flies, suggesting that evolution of TF specificities contributes to emergence of novel types of cells.

**Implications:** The conservation of TF specificity suggests that binding specificities are not subject to substantial evolutionary drift. Subtle binding preferences could change faster and independently of canonical DNA recognition, allowing TF to slowly drift in evolution.

**Conclusions:** This study extends the repertoire of Dila binding specificities by 122 high-resolution motifs and sheds light on features of Dila TF-binding characteristics. TFs belonging to the same family bind similar sites. We show that ortholog TF have identical DNA profiles, flanking sequences, and modes of binding, even though species diverged 600 million years ago. Our results suggest that evolution of TF specificities contributes to emergence of novel cell types.

**Methods:** We investigated the dynamics of hypermutation in Escheria coli exposed to high ethanol stress, a model of near-lethal stress. We used a collection of mutators with distinct mutation rates to identify optimal rates for rapid growth under ethanol and experimental evolution to reveal the role of hypermutation in adaptation.

**Findings:** Contrary to the notion that mutation rate is a slowly-evolving parameter, we demonstrate unexpected flexibility in cellular rates response to changes in selective pressure. Hypermutation arose concurrently with increases in ethanol concentrations, but rapidly declined once cells adapted to the stress. We identified cellular mortality as the major driving force behind the evolution of mutation rates.

**Implications:** Our findings shed new light on the dynamics of mutation evolution and explain the prevalence of hypermutation in various settings, including emergence of antibiotic resistance in microbes and cancer relapses upon chemotherapy. We show that organisms balance robustness and evolvability help maintain high mutation rates in time.

**Conclusion:** While mutation rate is generally considered a slowly-evolving parameter, our study reveals flexibility in cellular rates matching changes in selective to avoid near-lethal stress. Bacteria undergoing antibiotic treatment or cancer cells exposed to chemotherapy are examples of cells exposed to stressful conditions. Hypermutation quickly arose with increases in ethanol, but rates declined once cells adapted. We identified mortality as the major force driving evolution of mutation. Our results explain why maintaining high rates is limited in time.

**Key Findings:**

* Hypermutation arose with increases in ethanol, but declined once cells adapted
* Cellular mortality drives evolution of mutation
* Robustness and evolv help maintain high mutation rates
* Emergence of antibiotic resistance and cancer relapse explained by hypermutation

 ZIP's dimer activity is regulated by phosphorylation-triggered switch of dimer partners, regulating metabolic reprogramming.

**Research Question:** How do synapses communicate with the nucleus to regulate long-term potentiation and dendritic spine plasticity?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of R10, a novel synaptic protein, in bidirectional communication between synapses and nuclei, and its involvement in long-term potentiation (LTP) and dendritic spine plasticity.

**Findings:** We found that R10 is activated by calcium signals at the postsynaptic compartment and elicits changes at the transcriptional level. **Implications:** Our results indicate that R10 is a novel synaptic messenger responsible for long-lasting reaping of dendritic spines after specific synaptic stimuli and required for postsynaptic modifications to L induction. Impaired R10-mediated synuclear signaling and reduced R10 levels are responsible for dendritic density reduction following R10 silencing. Our findings suggest that the NAR complex is a source of messengers capable of trafficking to nucleus, encoding synaptic and extrasynaptic Gl2 NMD signals to nucleus and docking NAR-derived signalosome to nuclear sites in stimulus-dependent manner.

**Conclusions:** This study describes a novel syn-to-nucle signaling pathway linking synaptic activation of Gl2-containing MDs to gene expression. We demonstrate R10 is expressed in nucleus as well as synapses, part of NAR and directly interacts with cytoplasmic tail of Gl2 subunit of MDs. Our results provide new insights into the molecular mechanisms of LTP and dendritic plasticity, highlighting R10 as a key player in bidirectional communication between synapses and nuclei.

**Title:** Vestibular Control of Balance Transitions: Disengagement of Motor Policies during Motor State Transitions

**Research Question:** Do humans disengage vestibular stabilizing mechanisms before transitioning between motor states, as predicted by optimal feedback theory?

**Methods:** We investigated the vestibular control of balance during transitions between quiet standing and locomotion and between standing postures in healthy subjects. **Findings:** Discontinuities corresponding to coherence periods were observed preceding the onset of movement and during locomotion termination, indicating humans interrupt vestibular balance mechanisms to transition between motor states, suggesting a discrete change in motor control, as predicted by optimal theory.

**Implications:** Our results demonstrate that humans disengage vestibular stabilizing mechanisms before transitioning between motor states, indicating a general process that occurs between standing and other motor states. **Conclusions:** Our findings support the optimal theory that transitions require disengagement of motor control before implementation. The vestibular system encodes head motion in space and is vital for maintaining stability during standing and locomotion. Vestib signals influence activation of muscles in posture control. Prior to transitions, vestibular influence on whole-body responses is suspended. The suspension of vestibular-evoked responses at movement starts while subjects maintain upright, before transition. The suppression of vestibular stabilizing is not specific to one type, suggesting a general process. Our results show humans stop balancing before moving and moving before balancing again.

**Title:** Circadian Regulation of Carbon Metabolism in Cyanobacteria: The Role of the Transcription Factor RPA in Anticipating Periodic Energy Limitations

**Research Question:** How does the transcription factor RPA regulate circadian oscillations in cyanobacteria, driving genome-wide mRNA abundance, and what are the implications of its deletion on cell viability in constant and cycling light/dark conditions?

**Methods:** We investigated the role of the transcription factor RPA in regulating circadian oscillations in cyanobacteria by analyzing the effects of its deletion on cell viability in constant and cycling light/dark conditions. We examined the mechanisms underlying viability defects and demonstrated that the RPA- strain cannot maintain energy status at night, does not accumulate carbon reserves during the day, and is defective in transcription of genes for carbohydrate utilization at night.

**Findings:** Our results show that deletion of RPA has no effect on viability in constant light conditions but renders cells inviable in cycling conditions when light and dark alternate. We found that accumulation and utilization of carbon reserves for energy during periods of darkness are defective in RPA strain, and correction of these defects restores viability. Our results provide insight into the role of circadian pathway in enhancing fitness of cyanobacteria through coordination of central metabolism with metabolic demands imposed by periodic changes in environment.

**Implications:** Our findings suggest that circadian output mediated by RPA is required for anticipatory accumulation of carbon during day as well as metabolic switching to catabolism at dusk. These adaptations allow cells to stimulate energy and sustain viability during periods of darkness that constrain resources for cyanobacterial growth. Our data support a model in which anticipation of periodic-induced limitation is an essential role of cyanobacterial circadian system. The output of oscillator, through regulation of RPA, temporally coordinates metabolism to prepare cells for metabolic demands of periods.

**Conclusions:** Our study highlights the importance of circadian regulation in cyanobacterial metabolism, particularly in anticipation of periodic energy limitations. The transcription factor RPA, regulated by circadian clock, is critical for coordination of anabolism and catabolism and cell viability in light/dark cycles. Deletion of RPA prevents glycogen accumulation in light and renders cells unable to utilize glycogen at dusk, leading to alterations in cellular energy and previously observed reduction in fitness. Our results demonstrate the essential role of circadian system in enhancing cyanobacterial fitness through coordination of central metabolism with external demands.

**Implications:** These findings open avenues for drug discovery, as P-ATases function as virulence factors in many pathogens. **Conclusions:** This study provides the first structural and functional insights into P-4-ATases, a crucial for heavy-metal homeostasis and detoxification, and reveals novel features of heavy-metal transport. The results have implications for drug discovery and manipulation of heavy-metal accumulation in plants to prevent or enrich.

**Title:** Unveiling the Regulatory Mechanism of AAA+ Chaper Clp: Coiled-Coil Mediated Inhibition and Activation

**Research Question:** How do coiled-coil domains regulate the activity of the AAA+ chaper Clp, a central to bacterial virulence and stress resistance, and what are the implications of this regulatory mechanism?

**Methods:** We employed cryo-electron microscopy (cryo-EM) and biochemical assays to investigate the structure and function of Clp, a AAA chaper widely distributed among Gram-positive bacteria, and its interaction with the adaptor protein MecA.

**Findings:** We discovered that coiled-coil middle domains (MDs) of Clp control its activity by sequestering Clp in an inactive state, forming a large helical assembly via head-to-head contacts. MecA converts this resting state to an active planar structure by binding to MD sites, relieving repression. Loss of Clp repression in MD mutants causes constitutive activation and severe cellular toxicity.

**Implications:** Our findings reveal an unexpected regulatory concept by coiled-coil MDs controlling AAA+ chaper activity, extending their role as regulatory devices controlling AAA protein activity. This mechanism is distinct from other AAA chaper systems, such as ClB/Hsp, where coiled domains repress ATPase activity by forming a belt around the ring.

**Conclusions:** Coiled-coil domains of Clp regulate its activity by sequestering it in an inactive state, restricting substrate and adaptor binding, and ATP hydrolysis. Mutating a key MD residue causes cellular toxicity, suggesting this mechanism is operational in other AAA chaper systems, such as ClE and ClL, making them targets for antimicrobials.
